happiness than any other class of troubles that
affliefc mankind.

SATURDAY, MARCH 10, 1892.
Land Grant Railways.
Those two unfinished white elephants, the
transcontinental railway lines of South Aus-
tralia and Queensland, are once more attract-
ing the attention of the omnivorous British
financier. In each case a syndicate is desirous
of taking over the work and completing it
upon the land-grant principle, and, judging
from present appearances, the chances for
both syndicates are good. South Australia
has long been hungering to possess a railroad
right across the continent, and for many
years that impoverished province wasted
its substance in laying iron rails further
and further out into the tenantless wilder-
ness. It was a miserably unprofitable specu-
lation, when once the railway left the
confines of civilisation and stretched out into
regions where there was but one line-repairer
and a dingo to the square mile—where there
were neither passengers nor freight nor mails
nor any other kind of traffic whatsoever.
But the people of Adelaide had accepted it
as an axiom that their future depended on
the completion of the line, and they stuck to
the work manfully till the great depression
came, and their gigantic undertaking fell
down dead at Hergott Springs, with 1000
miles of empty desert still unbridged. Since
then the South Australians have sat discon-
solate among the ruins of their fallen ambi-
tion, but their morbid and unhealthy desire
for a great, useless, cumbersome, lonely
transcontinental line has never once abated.
As for the Queensland enterprise, it was
started under different auspices. M‘ll-
wraith advocated it first in his unregenerate
days, and conceived the great idea of having
it built by the agency of an English com-
pany, and there was to be a land-boom in
the North, and the greatest land-swindle
“Scotch colloquialism” —of the cen-
tury was looming up in perspective. Then
Griffith squashed the vast project, and
started to do the work on the old basis
by means of borrowed capital, but the job
died a natural death, for no one really
seemed to want the line or to have any use
for it whatsoever. After that MTlwraitii
found salvation, and made his calling and re-
election sure, and abjured nigger-labour and
land-grant railways together. And next he
lost his salvation again, and so did Griffith,
and to-day the Kanaka and the land-grant
system—both now under the patronage of
To.muel Griffilwraith —arc both looming
up once more'mi the immediate horizon, and
British syndicates are again negotiating to
secure the richest lands in the North gratis,
and be paid for taking them, and to pose as
public benefactors thereby.
• # »
The land grant system is based on the
assumption that Australia must continue to
build more railways in less time than any
other State in the civilised world, as the only
means of maintaining its prosperity. If it
cannot get them on the present system of
State-ownership, then it must do it by
giving away the monopoly to private specu-
lators, and if it cannot give away the mono-
poly, then the speculators must be paid to
take it. In either case it is accepted as a
self-evident axiom that the country must
keep on building new lines at a red-hot
pace. Australia, at the present time, has a
greater extent of railway, in proportion to
population, than any other country on the
face of the earth, with the one possible
exception of the United States, and the
cause of this immediate necessity is hard to
discover. Every lino that offers any reason-
able prospect of yielding a profit this
century has already been constructed, and
so, also, has every one which promises to be
only a moderate loss. The S.A. transcon-
tinental scheme is not wanted ; it is merely
a project for opening-up communication
where there is no one to communicate,
and for carrying passengers and freight
where there are neither freight nor passen-
gers to bo carried. There is something
imposing in the idea of building a steel
highway right across a continent, but so far
as present necessities are concerned it is just
as useful as the idea of opening public
libraries all round the Gulf of Carpentaria.
The Queensland scheme is still less
essential ; it is merely a device where-
by an utterly bankrupt nation may
be furnished with facilities for travelling into
a ghastly region of blight and malaria and
alligators, which hardly one single sane
human being really desires to visit. If any
ordinary inhabitant rvere deposited at the
proposed terminus of either line, he would
sooner walk back than stay there, and sooner
die than walk back. Australia doesn’t want
to give away a vast amount of valuable pro-
perty to induce any foreign monopolists to
build a line to either place ; it doesn’t want
to build the lines on the ordinary system of
State ownership ; it doesn’t even want to get
them for nothing. If it received two trans-
continental roads gratis it would find it
cheaper, under presentconditions, tosell them
for old iron than to work them. The frenzied
desire for railways everywhere, at any cost,
and utterly irrespective of population, is
just as insane as the old Dutch frenzy for
tulips, when roots fetched several hundred
pounds apiece. From this day forward Aus-
tralia needs to build its lines out of revenue
only, and to build them just as fast as
revenue will allow. 'When it will not allow,
the country should get out and walk.
* # *
The ostensible object of the present system
of railway construction is to “open up the
country.” In other words, the country con-
structs, at a vast expenditure of borrowed
money, a new lino through some howling
waste, and is thus enabled to sell the land
along the route, which would otherwise be, for
the time, unsaleable. The entire proceeds of
the land are absorbed in the course of years
to pay interest on the cost of construction,
and the loss on working expenses comes out
of the consolidated revenue. The country
thus, through its haste, gives away the public
estate to buy a dead loss and an incubus, and
is worse off than if it had never owned either
the land or the incubus. The “ opening up
of the country ” makes rooixx for new hordes
of British paupers- who are sometimes
xictually imported at the cost of the State, in
order that the newly-opexxed regions may not
remain tenantless ; and lands which will be
urgently wanted to provide room for the
coming generations of Australians are
muddled away at great expense so as to
hasten, in the name of progress, the time
when our sons will be overcrowded and
hoxneless in their ownr country. To get rid
of the public estate by this process it costs,
probably, from first to last, nearly twice as
nxuch as that estate is worth, and among
these nxultiplied losses there is oxxe, and only
one, item of profit - the nation in the end is
one railway ahead. It may be a dead loss
for 20 years, but at all events it is there.
When everything else is gone in financial
riot and confusion, the great white elephant
at least remains.
# # *
This is the Australian system as applied to
such outlying lines as the two great tx’ans-
continental highways. The land grant device
is a modification of the same principle, the
difference consisting chiefly in the fact that
the country, in this instance, does not get
the railway. Dix-ectly or indix’ectly the
nation suffers all the losses attendant on the
ordinary plan of State-construction and State-
ownership, and the only asset in the whole
transaction goes to the syndicate. The methods
of the British speculators who propose to
take up the unfinished enterprise at Hergott
Springs are beautiful in their simplicity.
They ax*e to x-cccive, in the first place, an
immense ax-ea of land —almost all the really
valuable property, in fact, along the lino.
The capital required for construction pur-
poses w ill be raised by mortgaging this land
in England—the speculators themselves do
not propose to put up one single coin from
first to last. When the line is finished,
therefore, the syndicate will possess 1000
miles of railway and an estate as largo as a
good-sized kingdom, less the mortgage lia-
bility ; and they will have received these
two assets for absolutely nothing. And as
that mortgage is secured upon the soil of
Australia, this country will owe the amount
as absolutely as if it had been raised on
Government debentures. If the value of
the line is £10,000,000, then the situation,
at the conclusion of the work, would, under
the system of Government ownership, be
this: the State would owe £10,000,000,
and possess an asset which cost the same
amount as a set-off. Under the syndicate
system the country would have parted with
property worth at least £15,000,000 —it must
be worth that amount else no syndicate
could reckon confidently on raising
£10,000,000 upon the security—and would
have nothing whatever as compensation.
The primary recommendation of the land-
grant system is that it provides means of com-
munication—generally in some inaccessible
region where no sane man really wants to
communicate—without inflicting a burden on
the nation ; the reality is that it greatly in-
creases the burden on the nation, while some
foreign speculators get the entire assets.
The British monopolist kindly proposes to
take the land and the transcontinental rail-
way without paying anything for either, and
therefore it is obvious that somebody must
pay, and the Australian people, as the only
other parties to the nefarious transaction,
are necessarily the ones who will bear the
liability.
• # *
The new line, in the hands of the State,
would doubtless be a loss for at least the
first decade. On the State railways the
people practically fix the rates, and as they
generally fix them below the paying limit
the result is a chronic deficiency. Then the
deficiency is made good out of the consoli-
dated revenue, and the nation pays its own
bill in another shape. The syndicate, having
no consolidated revenue to fall back upon,
will charge such rates as will make the line
self-supporting ; and thus the people, having
first given away the land, and secondly
given away the railway, will make good
all after deficiencies for the British speculator
just as they would otherwise do for them-
selves. The syndicate’s land Avill necessarily
be sold to all comers in order to create
traffic. Some of it Avill doubtless be traded
off’in this country, and someAvill be disposed
of, by dint of much hard lying, to foreign
settlers. If General Booth chooses to pur-
chase, and desires to settle his thousands of
broken-doAvn criminals on the transcontinen-
tal line, or Hiesch wants to exj ort his host
of unclean Russian Jews, the local Govern-
ment will have no option save to admit
these obnoxious hordes ; having authorised
the syndicate to sell it cannot reasonably
shut out the purchasers, Avhoever they may
be. To all intents and purposes the Govern-
ment will have surrendered its right to
exclude objectionable colonists ; Avhen the
syndicate has had time to consolidate its
political influence—after the manner of the
railroad magnates Avho now run the United
States and carry the Senate in their carpet-
bags—and has duly bought up the Legisla-
ture, even the Chinamen will find an
entrance without much difficulty. The
reason of at least one-half the railroads that
are now constructed is to absorb surplus
labour, but the English company will,
almost to a certainty, import its labour—
paitly for the sake of cheapness, partly
Avith a view to settling its employes along
the line and selling its allotments to them.
Sundry politicians will get a share in the
profits, and there the advantage will end.
Queensland and South Australia will have
sold their souls for a transcontinental line,
and both the souls and the line will go to
the omnivorous syndicate.
* * *
And at the end we come back to the grue-
some anomaly Avith which we started—there
is no demand for these ruinous Avorks what-
soever. They are to be constructed in a
lonesome wilderness Avhich no more requires
a railway at the present time than it does a
marble cathedral. The lines Avill open-up
the country, but they will open it up for the
syndicate only, and Avhereas the Australians
now own the land in an undeveloped state,
they will then cease to own it altogether.
There is not one redeeming feature in the
Avhole unmitigated swindle. lire money
which the British company borrows to carry
out its enterprise will,be orved by, and must
ultimately be paid by, Australia, just as truly
as if it had been borrowed on Government
debentures, for the company will meet its
debentures with money extracted somehow
from this country, and the Government
could do no more. The loss Avhich would
naturally ensue through running a super-
fluous means of conveyance in a thinly-
peopled country will be made good by the
nation as much as if the work were its own,
for the company must get its revenue out of
Australia or burst, and a fraud of such
dimensions seldom bursts. The profits,
Avhen they begin, will go to England.
Having already paid for its railway, this
country, if it wants to own it, must buy it
from the company and pay for it a second
time in cash. If it wants to recover the
lands which it gave away, and to abolish
landlordism, it must buy them back—pro-
bably at three times their original value,
and their original value being probably 50
per cent, over that of the line, this will be
equivalent to paying for that famous institu-
tion four-and-a-half times more. And having
thus paid for its transcontinental road nearly
six times over, the country will be six times
Averse off than if it had constructed that
famous monument of progress on its own
responsibility in the first instance! and also
much woise off than if there had never been
any such monument at all. Truly there is a
great future in store fur the land-grant
railway industry.
A Vote for “Fixity.”
Epps, the “ cokum ” man, who explains U)ings
for the New South Wales “ National” Associa-
tion, is still doing his fatal work in the daily
papers, and unless the Association can hire
somebody to explain Epps, that gentleman’s
talent for ethical confusion will presently bring
his employers to grief. It is one of the truly
fiendish characteristics of this particular gather-
ing of capitalists that it is always expounding
its political platform, and its expositions are
just as clear as might be expected from an idiot
head downwards in a mud-hole. When it com-
menced its career under the asgis of Tommy Kelly,
the rich and dignified ex-storeman in theglitter-
ingly new Avhitetile, the Association advocated,
openly and boldly, the retention of the present
system of plural voting as a reward for the
ownership of land or bricks and mortar, and it
called this a means for the encouragement of
Thrift. It assumed, with the fatuousness of
the Epps who rushes in where angels fear to
tread, (1) that land and houses are always got
by Thrift and never by inheritance, by swin-
dling, open robbery, gambling, lying, land-
jumping, or political treachery; (2) t hat the
want of land and houses is always conclusive
evidence of want of Thrift, and that poverty
never arises through sickness, low wages, want
of work, the possession of a large family,
through being swindled in a bank, or building
society, or financial company, through drought,
combustion, lightning, hail, floods, pestilence,
sudden death, excessive good-nature, or any
other cause whatever ; (3) that Thrift is
the only virtue worth encouraging, and
that benevolence, filial affection, honesty,
patriotism and all the rest are not worth men-
tioning ; (4) that land and houses are the only
possible evidence of Thrift; and (5) that the
thrifty man is always a good law-maker and the
thriftless man a bad one. These were the theories
on which the association travelled in the early
stages of its career, but when Epps had explained
himself concerning them, his taskmasters broke
out in an altogether fresh place. Therefore,
Epps writes to the papers now to suggest that
every man should have one vote, that the
thrifty man should have one extra vote, and that
the evidences of thrift which shall qualify for
that additional suffrage shall be as follows :
Any evidence of a man’s thrift should be included under
the term. With the squatter’s station should be included
the artisan’s cottage, savings bank account, life insurance
policy, a Crown lease or section, length of residence for a
certain term in one tenement or electorate (which indi-
cates fixity and stability), and a thousand and one other
matters, all of which are evidences of a man’s 1, -ake or
interest in the country.
* * *
No doubt Epps, as he goes horribly on his
Avay down the dreary columns of the S.M.
Hkkald, is quite serious in the suggestion that
a man should have an extra vote for living a
long time in one house, because it “ indicates
fixity and stability.” And it never dawns
upon him that it may mean not stability, but
only paralysis. A paralytic generally hangs
out for a great length of time in one house,
because lie is difficult to move. So docs a
dropsical patient, and a person without legs,
and a bedridden individual with a small
annuity. They have no more “stability ” than
the average of humanity, but they move with
difficulty, and are inert by nature. This trait
is one which they share with the homicide, who
demonstrates bis fixity by being for a great
length of time in one gaol, or the lunatic who
remains for 42 jears in the same asylum. The
homicide and the lunatic would undoubtedly
be entitled to a vote for “ fixity Columbus,
if he were alive to-day, would not —he was an
unstable and unfixed person, who would have
lost his vote by going away to discover
America. The same disability would attend
pretty nearly every other man who ever left a
huge and indelible footprint on the sands of
time—every apostle who stormed the world
with a new creed, every great missionary,
soldier, explorer, politician, writer—for no man
can write well who has not seen the world,
every artist —unless he is willing to spend his
whole life in painting the view from his frofit
window, every zoologist, botanist, geologist—
everybody, in short, who has ever done any-
thing worth remembering or recording. There
is no stability or fixity among them all.
And why Epps should want people to be
“fixed” in this unnatural manner is difficult
to imagine. For some unexplainable reason he
desires to confer a double share of political power
on the fossil who has locked the same back door
after putting out the same cat every night for
twenty years—who has grown thick-headed and •
blue-moulded through looking out of the same
window at the same back yard—who has dozed
in the same chimney-corner, and got his beer
from the same public-house, and gabbled to the
same neighbour over the same fence, and bought
his gory beefsteak from the same butcher for
two decades or more. In what way the voter
is improved by this long era of dull, dead
monotony, or how Epps is the better for it. are
two absolutely insoluble problems. ■
» • «
Even granting, however, that a “ fixed ”
person is better, in some mysterious way, than
one who is not fixed, and that the stable voter
has lived for many years in one house from high
patriotic motives, and not because the corpse of
his first wife’s aunt is buried and bricked in
under the hearth-stone and he is afraid to move
lest the next tenant should find her bones, and
assuming that Epps has a right to interfere in
the matter, it still remains to be seen whether
this particular test of fixity is the true one.
The man who has lived long in one tenement
may be “stable,” but all stability does not
necessarily develope in this direction. The
person who hasn’t had his hair cut for a long
time also manifests stability in another form,
and so does the one who has neglected to change
his collar for a lengthened period. The man
who has pawned his watch regularly for many
years with thesame“ uncle,” and the one who has
taken in the same paper for a whole generation,
and the individual who has held on to the same
batch of unprofitable mining scrip longer than
anybody else, and the heavy, melodramatic
father who has turned his daughter out of doors
and kept her out for a great length of time, are
all characters who arc gifted with much fixity,
and ought to have an extra vote in consequence.
The poet who sends the same rejected mass o *


verse for years round the whole of the Austra-
lian press, and finally, in his old age, inserts it
as an advertisement, is the most fixed indivi-
dual we know of, and compared to him even the
gentleman who lives in one house for four
centuries is loose, unreliable, and shaky. The
monomaniac is also a person of great stability,
and the manner in which he dings to his
one delusion assuredly deserves special
recognition. The living curse who sings
one song all his life and generally
sings it horribly—is at least as worthy of
reward as the inhabitant who vegetates to a
great age without shifting his residence, and
the dull, fat man—we know him well—who
has made the same joke every day for 30 years,
is also deserving of consideration. Further,
we put in a plea for the man who doesn’t change
his linen ; if the fossil who stays for ever in one
tenement thereby earns the gratitude of his
country, the one who stays for ever in one shirt
should not be forgotten. Fixity, at the best, is
a poor, mean kind of virtue, rather better than
hiccup and a shade worse than ordinary ignor-
ance, but even at the worst it is advisable to
make sure that the right kind of fixity is
rewarded and not an altogether sham or bogus
variety. A bit of brown paper is not much to
whoop over, but a counterfeit bit of brown
paper is still worse, and it is plainly the counter-
feit article which Epps has got hold of.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Desertions, Grass-Widows, and Bigamy.
Last year, in the city of Sydney and its
suburbs, there were celebrated some 3500 mar-
riages. In the same year and the same district,
according to the report of the Benevolent
Asylum authorities, there were ex-celebrated
623 wife-desertions, that being the number
reported to the police; allowing for husband
desertions, and cases where the neglected
female did not report the matter to the authori-
ties, the number might, in all probability, be
safely estimated at 800 or 900. This is a
serious item to go against the marriage record,
and on a rough estimate it would appear that
in the New South Wales metropolis and its
environs every fourth husband bolts habitually
and leaves his family unprovided for. The
Sydney Daily Telegraph, with its customary
incapacity for arithmetic, disputes this calcula-
tion, on the ground that “ the number of
deserters in a year are not drawn from the
number of couples married during that period,
but from the whole population,” but this only
asserts that a husband seldom runs away in the
first year of his marriage ; the one indisputable
fact is that, so long as there are 3500 marriages
and 800 or 900 desertions per annum, one
husband out of every four mysteriously van-
ishes, or elopes, or otherwise goes away and
fails to come back. These surprising figures
open up a lot of probabilities. In Sydney,
every tenth person is a married—or nominally
married —-female, and thus every fortieth per-
son is a deserted wife. Allowing for her
absconding husband and the four deserted
children—which is below the real average
—it would thus appear that one person
in every eight has either unlawfully
bolted or been unlawfully bolted from. The
number of husbands who disappear from the
city of Sydney and its environs is, according
to these calculations, nearly twice as many as
the number who die, therefore, when any rash
individual marries an alleged widow, it is at
the very least an even chance that the lady is
committing bigamy—it might even be put
down as a two to one chance, but we are
anxious to err on the safe side. This is a
serious drawback, and should be duly con-
sidered by all men who want to marry widows.
Similarly, the lady who marries a widower
takes an ecpial risk, which is another serious
drawback. The number of accumulated
bigamists who are loose upon the soil of New
South Wales must be something tremendous,
and the fact that the marriage-rate of the
province is higher than that of any European
State, with the single exception of Hungary,
is probably accounted for by this unnatural
tendency of the population to keep on marrying
regardless of expense. These estimates are, of
course, based on the assumption that each hus-
band only absconds once, and that each wife is
only once deserted. .There are, no doubt,
hardened or habitual vanishers wdio have left a
■whole succession of disconsolate wives, and
committed bigamy over and over again, and on
the other hand there are probably certain
ladies who, owing to ugliness, great power of
jaw, bad cookery, fits, extravagance, drink, or
a tendency to stay out late at night, have been
quitted by a long series of husbands ; and these
may reduce, in some degree, the actual number
of absconders, grass-widows, and bigamists,
though without, in any way, reducing the
aggregate criminality. So far, no one seems to
have advanced any reasonable theory as to how
this great difficulty is to be met. There are no
visible signs or evidences by which the law-
breaker can be detected in time, and, to all
appearance, the process of wife-desertion will
go on throughout Australia until the continent
is filled with bigamists, and widows who are
not widows, and alleged bachelors who are not
bachelors, and infants who have been left on
a cold, hard world, unfriended and alone,
ft every bride and bridegroom were to be
solemnly branded by the parson or the registrar
with a red-hot poker, somewhere between the
shoulder-blades, it might possibly meet the
difficulty, but such a remedy would probably
be found too severe. And the only other
course seems to be to let things slide, and to
allow everybody to marry as often as he or she
may f ee j inclined —which will, doubtless, be
pretty frequently.

fiiiK National Mutual Life Association of Aus-
tralia was, in The Bulletin’s opinion, unwise
to defend the action brought against it by the
widow of a client who had insured for £4OO, on
_bo ground that the insurer, while suffering
rom an internal tumour, had declared himself
J V e > 80 for as ho knew, in good health, he
having also been passed as a “good life” by
Die Association’s doctor. The rational principle
that should guide life-insurance societies seems
to be that they should face all risks and conse-
quences once they have accepted a policy and
taken its current premium. Every experienced
doctor professionally disbelieves, or at least
doubts, pretty nearly everything that a
patient tells him. Insurance-societies should
recognise this common idiosyncrasy and
deal with it as a matter of pure business,
according to the now well - understood
law of probabilities. After all, the question
resolves itself into a problem of actuarial
averages, and, when thus scientifically dealt
with, there is no more risk of loss on the yross
insurances in insuring (say) 1000 sick people
than there is in insuring 1000 apparently
healthy persons. A year or two ago, when the
Queensland Government proposed to enforce
compulsory life-insurance on every member of
its Civil Service, one big insurance company
offered special terms for the privilege of being
allowed to insure the lot, sickly and strong,
young and old, paralytic and springy, without
medical examination, it being held that the
observance of a scientific law of averages would
make the enterprise not only safe, but remunera-
tive. A life policy, once granted, should be as
indefeasible as a promissory-note, and should be
made so by law. Until this reform is estab-
lished and rigidly adhered to, insurance com-
panies must remain more or less objects of
popular distrust.
The numerous Australian law-students, who
are “ eating dinners ” in London to qualify for
the English bar, are “much disheartened” at
the new Victorian Amalgamation Act, under
which a solicitor is virtually also a barrister
and a barrister is additionally a solicitor :
If a certificate of call to the English bar is no longer
sufficient to qualify for admission to the Victorian bar,
the students hold that it is waste of time to come home
(meaning h ingland) to study, as it would be infinitely
better to be articled in the first instance to a colonial
barrister.
Hear, hear. If the Victorian Act effects no
other result than the establishment of an inde-
pendent Australian bar, that seeks no foreign
imprimatur, we shall have much to be thankful
for.
The gorgeous confusion of the S.M. Herald :
What, then, is a Freetrader? Is he a man who would
be willing to collect moderate Customs duties under a
simple tariff for the sake of revenue only, supplementing
them, if necessary, for the maintenance of the revenue, by
duties of excise? Or is he one who would shut up the
Custom-house and endeavour to carry on Government by
confiscating private property in land under the pressure
of a Single-tax? The first of these senses is the one in
which, with a desire to avoid confusion, the term should
be used.
That’s just it. A Freetrader is a man who says
that there should be no Customs duties in
theory, but he collects them “moderately ” in
practice so as “ to avoid confusion.” And the
man who has to act directly contrary to his
own principles so as to avoid confusion must
have a very confused set of principles anyhow.
Last week, in Pentridge Prison, four men and
one boy were tied up and skinned alive by the
Government man-flayer. One man was a grand-
fatherly victim ; the boy was 16 years of age.
It is argued that the lash acts as a deterrent to
the commission of violent crimes, and that the
lash, and the lash only, put down a London
garotting epidemic. P. A. Taylor has fully
proved by official statistics that the volume,
and even the ratio, of violent crimes has by no
means decreased in England since the adoption
of the lash ; brutality is specially inherent in
the Anglo-Saxon, as witness British mainten-
ance of judicial torture. The cat finds many
Australian, and pre-eminently many Victorian,
supporters merely because it has English sanc-
tion. England stands alone among civilised
people in punitive barbarity. She has as her
comrades-in-brutality only Russia, China,
Turkey, Persia, the Red- Indians, and other
barbaric or semi-barbaric peoples. She is
welcome to her pride in her allies. No French-
man, no German, no Italian, no Scandinavian,
no Swiss, Dutchman, Austrian, American—
save the wife-beater of the little State of
Delaware —may be legally flogged m his own
country. The lash is distinctly a Tory torture,
invented for, and only applied to, the man in
the garb of the workman. No one ever saw a
man in broadcloth and moving in society sen-
tenced to be flogged, no matter how brutal may
have been his offence. On this point, so far as
Australia goes, The Bulletin invites contra-
diction. The cat is a special reservation for
the torture of the “ lower orders.” That alone
should ensure its abolition by a democracy.

The London papers report another evidence of
the blessedness of “freedom of contract.” A
great printing firm—the Rose Court Printing
Co. —is the cause of the trouble, and after
years of incessant slave-driving it has acciden-
tally been found out. On the particular occasion
to which attention is drawn 31 girls commenced
work at eight o’clock on Thursday morning
and worked till a quarter to five on Friday
afternoon. They had one or two short breaks
for meals, and were allowed to lie down and
snatch one or two hours’ sleep on the-floor, but
for 33 hours they never quitted the factory.
Then, after eight hours’rest, they resumed work
at midnight and toiled continuously for 13 hours
more, till one o’clock on Saturday afternoon.
The wages for this sort of labour were Bs. a
week, without any extra allowance for overtime.
No compulsion, as the company carefully points
out, was ever put upon the girls : they
had absolute freedom to refuse to work beyond
the regulation hours. All the same, not one of
them did refuse; the dismal privilege of earn-
ing Bs. a week was too precious to bo hazarded
by any sign of unwillingness. Not one of them
complained to the Inspector of Factories ; the
law was ready to protect them whenever
appealed to, but if anyone did appeal then her
Bs. a week would promptly vanish into the
air, and, moreover, she would be black-listed
by all other factory-owners as a dangerous and
cantankerous person who raised industrial dis-
turbances. The girls didn’t even want the law to
come to their assistance of its own accord, for if
it did someone must necessarily be summoned as
a witness, and the fact of giving evidence, no
matter how unwillingly, would almost certainly
prove fatal to the billet and the miserable lit tle
salary. They were all absolutely willing, irt
fact, to work 33 hours at a stretch—they would
doubtless have been willing to work 48 hours if
necessary. This is “ freedom of contract ” when
it gets down to the bed-rock. There is no com-
pulsion whatever; everybody is at absolute
liberty—to starve. The situation is just the
same as that of the free citizen at the bottom of
an ice-well in an uninhabited land. He is free
to stay in, and he is also free to come out; in
every respect he is absolutely untrammelled.
But the sides of the ice-well are very steep and
inaccessible, and he generally stays in. He is
also free to feel warm and comfortable, but he
usually freezes all the same.
During the recent examination of a Sydney
insolvent, the examinee admitted having taken
certain furniture to be upholstered to the extent
of over £2O worth of work and material, after
which he assumed the line of defence that
the furniture was the property of his wife.
There had been a bill-of-sale over it, and,
after the upholstering, a fresh advance of £3O
was obtained on it, and a new bill given in lieu
of the old one. The exasperated upholsterer
inveighed bitterly to the Court against the
rascality of a man who declared that he gave
the order on his own behalf, while swearing
that the furniture was his wife’s property. The
upholsterer seems to have legally “accepted
payment ” by taking the man’s bill. The broad,
just rule should be that, where a wife’s separate
estate or property benefits or is improved by
work done, or material supplied, the wife should
be held personally responsible for the debt so
incurred, as it is to be assumed, in every case,
that she is cognisant of what happens to the
belongings in her custody. Years ago, in
Richmond (Melb.), the present writer saw a
wife successfully sued for the value of a quantity
of paint, purchased by her husband in his own
name, but applied to premises of which she
claimed to be the owner.
When Premier Griffith landed the other day
instate at Rockhampton (Q.) a crowd of his
own particular admirers met him on the wharf
and cheered. There was only one discordant
note in the symphony, and that was supplied by
a certain John Willis, who didn’t feel like
cheering and groaned instead. He was
promptly arrested, and when he appeared at
the Court next day the police conducted the
prosecution with much vigour, and demanded
that he should be committed for trial. As it
chanced, however, the magistrate failed to see
that Griffith should be exempt from public
criticism, and after a long and dreary mass of
evidence had been waded through, the prisoner
was discharged. What The Bulletin is now
waiting to hear about is the arrest of the
balance of the crowd —the ones who applauded.
If the case rested on the question of “ dis-
orderly conduct,” then cheering is quite as
disorderly as groaning, and makes at least 18
times more row, and therefore people cheering in
the public streets ought to be remorselessly
prosecuted. If it didn’t rest on the matter of
“ disorderly conduct,” then what did it rest on?
The one party, presumably, cheered because it
liked Griffith ; the other, apparently, groaned
because it didn’t like Griffith, and as there is
no law, even in Queensland, requiring that
everybody shall like Griffith, logic demands
that, if any arrests were made, the whole crowd
should be run in together. If the grovelling
police official who seized upon this solitary dis-
sentient wished to lay down the law that only
one side of the case should be heard, and that
the other side should lie low under pain of
arrest, then he ought to run in the whole of
the Queensland Opposition, for it also doesn’t
appreciate Premier Samuel, and the language
it aims at him is stronger than all the groans
in the province. It is no good to talk about
freedom of speech if the nation is only free to
say what Griffith wants and nothing else. If
the Queensland Cabinet has any shred of intelli-
gence left in its composition it ■will promptly
sack the Rockhampton boss-Bumble from the
force as a man who is unfit to hold office, and
hardly fit to be at large, in a civilised country.
The policeman who goes about arresting people
for not liking Samuel Griffith is altogether
too vast an autocrat for Australia.
There is no “ imprisonment for debt ” in New
South Wales ! J. T. Merry lost a law-suit in
Sydney, some months ago, and was ordered to
pay over £387. He didn’t. On 22nd Dec.
last, a fresh order was made that Merry should
pay the amount into Court within three weeks.
Again he didn’t. Last week Merry, “attached,”
appeared before Judge Owen in custody. He
promised to discharge the debt as soon as he
could, saying that he was now in a position to
go into business at Eden. His Honor quietly sent
Merry to the debtors’ prison, saying that he had
no course open except to commit him to Dar-
linghurst until that unfortunate “purged his
contempt by payment of the amount,” and that
defendant’s only chance of Eden was to arrange
with plaintiff. Merry, therefore, is not im-
prisoned for “debt”—thank Humbug!—but
only for “ contempt of court,” and is placed
beyond the means of earning money so that he
may the more speedily pay money. There is
no imprisonment for debt in New South Wales.
But Merry’s body is in gaol, and a creditor,
whose slave and captive he now is, holds the
key of his cell.

There is a moral aspect about the New South
Whiles Government Labour Bureau which is
instructive. The institution is haunted by the
detectives of Sydney, who are always on the
lookout for discharged prisoners, and when
they find that one of this objectionable class
has registered himself as an applicant for
honest employment he is promptly reported,
and a black mark is put opposite his name.
The Bureau objects to providing work for dis-
charged prisoners ; so, for that matter, does
every other institution ; and if by any chance
the ex-offender should find employment, despite
this handicap, some hulking police official
promptly “reports ” him to his employer, and
(such is human nature) obtains his discharge.
An Australian detective spends half his time
going round on the trail of discharged prisoners
to prevent them reforming, and when he has
successfully blocked thcex-confinee from getting
any job, however humble, he arrests him for
not having a job; ami the magistrate admonishes
the prisoner in the dock, and tells him that ha
ought to work, and gives him three months
gaol, and when he comes out again the same
process is repeated with only one variation —
that the prisoner gets six months on the second
occasion instead of three. When an offender
has served his sentence the police have abso-
lutely no more right to interfere with
him than they have to interfere with a
bank-manager or a Premier, but until
a dozen detectives are sent to penal servitude
for conspiring to prevent a free citizen obtain-
ing honest employment the force will go on in
its old, brutal, unreasoning fashion. It never
interferes with the rich swindler who has served
his sentence and settled down to enjoy the pro-
ceeds of his fraud ; with the natural instincts
of its mean little soul it reserves all its atten-
tion for the small, penniless offender. So far as
concerns the Labour Bureau, at all events, the
Dibbs Government ought to suppress this un-
lawful system of tyranny and blackmail on the
instant. That institution was created at the
cost of the nation to furnish an equal chance of
employment for any man who is willing to
work. When it is used as an engine to prevent
the reformation of the discharged criminal, then
it becomes an outrage upon morality and an
indelible disgrace to everybody concerned.

Dear Bulletin,— Apropos your remarks
with regard to the travellers recently imprisoned
for asking for food, let me recall an incident
of my own bluey-humping experience. One
day while tramping in the Tarcutta district,
in company with an old Bendigo celebrity known
as Jim Reddan, we got on the wrong track and
were all day without food. Towards evening we
came upon a tilted cart with a fire alongside.
Seated by the fire was an overgrown German,
reading a newspaper, and, most welcome sight of
all, was a huge damper lying on a stump close
by. We both had money—so I immediately
asked Fatherland if he would sell us some tucker.
“ Don’t haf got no ducker,” he replied sullenly.
“ No tucker,” said I, eyeing the damper wistfully;
“well, then, sell me some bread.” “Don't haf
got no pread,” he again answered, more sullenly
than before. I was in despair, we were famish-
ing, but my mate Jim came to the rescue in
a most decisive manner. “ You say you have no
bread ?” queried Jim, looking straight at the sulky
German. “No! no pread,” growled the other,
with persistent mendacity, “nod a bid for my
own subber.” ♦“ Not a bit for your own supper !”
echoed Jim ; “ poor devil! well, I’ll be blanked if
I’ll see any man without bread for his supper,”
and he strode up to the stump, seized the damper,
ran his sheath-knife across the middle of it, and
graciously presented the dumbfoundered Teuton
with half his own damper, coolly observing as he
did so, “ I don’t like to see any blanky man stuck.
Here, old man, here’s some bread for your supper;”
then, with a “Come along, mate,” he tucked the
other half under his arm and walked off, leaving
the German speechless with amazement. It is no
sin to steal food. —Yours, G.B.
The Bega (N.S.W.) Gazette ;
The Bulletin’s weak point comes to the front when our
cynical friend essays dealing with statistics. In a par
last week The Bulletin’s scissors snipped on some facts
about savings banks, and it lets its printer put in type
how England was bursting up because her savings banks
deposits were only £67,000,000 from 4,000,000 depositors,
whilst 6,500,000 French depositors had £112,000,000 stored
up. But we are not told how in France the military and
naval expenditure for 1887 was 39} millions ; for Britain,
25} ; or how from 1882 to 1889 France wasted on her
army and navy (more than the huge empire of Russia)
230 J millions, Great Britain £1634. Or how the French
revenue taxed from the people was, in 1887, 129 J millions
against Britain’s £93}; whilst the indirect taxation in
France for that year was £ss} millions, in Britain
£44}. Or that France had to pay on her public debt
interest amounting to £39l} millions, or 20s. 6d.
per head of her population, Britain £22} millions, or 12s.
7d. per head. And we are told nothing of British Banks,
with a paid-up capital of £70,000,000 for the Joint Stock
Banks alone, and holding deposits worth £6OO millions.
The Bulletin is obliged to the Bega man for
emphasising its case. France has established
Protection, abolished monarchy, and wiped out
the entire race of big landlords ; and, as a result,
althouf/h her people have to pay every year
£14,000,000 more for military and naval purposes,
and £17,000,000 more for interest on the public
debt than those of Britain, and though they lost
at least £500,000,000 from first to last in the war
of 1870-71, and despite the fact that the best of
the nation’s manhood is away on military service,
still the savings banks —the banks of the poor—
have 2} million more depositors and £45,000,000
more deposits than those of England ! If France
were an island like England, then it could dis-
pense with its huge fortresses and its army of
3,000,000 men, and save the £31,000,000 per
annum that Providence has saved to England, and
then it would bo further ahead even than
it is. The £600,000,000 deposits in the
English Joint-Stock banks are nothing to
the point. These are the deposits of
huge capitalists like the Duke of Westminster
and his kind, and have as little to do with the
case as a starving peasant evicted in the snow has
with a marble cathedral. The matter in question
is that Republicanism, Protection, and the aboli-
tion of large estates have ameliorated poverty in
France, and that the working-classes, despite the
conscription and the tremendous load of debt, are
vastly better off than they arc in the “ free ” and
miserable land of England.

My Dear Moorabinua, —
A recent Sydney divotce-case re-pointed the moral that
women do not (as the Suffragists insist) get all the kicks
and none of the ha’pence which are scooped in by
almighty' Man, but are treated with greater consideration
in many ways. As an instance: in the race for tire
parsley-wreath mentioned in the van of these stirring
truths, a medical question came up, and a fusillade of
impertinent personal queries was fired at the petitioning
husband by the counsel for the defence until the Judge
even was moved to pity. Now it seems that when a
woman is made a target of in the witness-box and the
opposing barrister wants to—er— itemize her doctor’s
bills, it is the rule to put the questions privately in
writing, and if that isn’t a concession worth being a
woman to get I would like to know what is? Poor
Married Man has to loudly describe, if needs be, the
longtitude and latitude of every mustard-plaster he has
been flayed with, in answer to the hoarse questions of a
wigged tormentor in open court. Woman is per-
mitted to softly state what she took for the earache
(or as the case might be) in faint and blushing pencil.
It isn’t a joyous thing to be a man quite all the time,
is it ?
Apropos of divorce—a Sydney woman of a charitable
disposition had a brush with a divorcee quite recently.
She (the divorcee) had limed another matrimonial bird
"ho tried to get her whitewashed by imploring his lady
friends to call upon the wronged darling who was quietly-
living it all down poor dear. One went. On the visit the
darling behaved quite up to the part, but when she paid
her return visit, she made her way to the good Samaritan’s
drawingroom with a hat on three hairs, and a pug-dog
under each elbow.
By the way, I see that a large number of the Suffragistg
want the N.S.W. Government to appoint female gaolers
for the benefit of women in country prisons. Can it be
that the lady-agitators pant for billets under the regis of
that fetching bachelor, Comptroller-General Miller?
Some of them - the moustachioed ones—would certainly
make mest able-bodied penal officials.
In choosing a husband (that brilliant manner of launch-
ing a pearl of wisdom reminds me of the first plunge of a
cook-book recipe) the sensible young woman who is lucky-
enough to have the chance will do well not to throw her
handkerchief at the “jolly” man whose kiss is a loud-
resounding smack (though, mind you, I haven’t the
faintest idea'of how she’s going to find that out) and who
leads the convivial " He a jolly good f ler ! ” chorus round
a ball suppor table, because he is invariably the interest-
ing gentleman who figures in the nisi prim division
charged with splitting the door panels with bis gristly
fist and pursuing his wife with a shot-gun under the
glimmering starlight six months after the wedding.
It’s nearly always the “ fine, jovial, rattling good
fellow ” who is a perfect brute by his own ingle, and don’t
let Virginia forget it. It is generally safe to bet that the
Benedict whom the barmaid hastens to serve has just
left a wife who is turning the trimming on her last
Autumn’s bonnet, or is patching Johnny’s trousers with
pa’s worn-out dress-coat, and while her legal supporter is
calling for letter champagne down tow n, she moistens
the parched side of her soul at the pump. Oh, maidens
wistful for matrimony (I’m repeating Just what all the
married women tell me)tfo keep your “ weather eye ” on
the “popular ” male defendant in a dozen divorce-suits
and thereby learn to deprecate the rollicking suitor who
wears his hat a little to loo’ard and is worshipped and
quoted by a string of male devotees ! You may not, of
course, deserve a better husband than this expansive soul
will make, but that is no reason why I shouldn’t raise a
hollow, moaning voice and warn you unto seventy timts
seven against him.
I am not as a rule interested in murder cases, but the
story of how the Windsor assassin—almost directly after
murdering his wife—went to a Melbourne matrimonial
agency in the character of a would -be bridegroom
compels one’s horrified interest. How careful women
should be when entering into matrimonial engagements
with men of whom they know absolutely nothing—men
who, for ought they know, may be (as in the present case)
giving them as a pledge of affection the opal brooch
taken from the throat of some woman bricked-up beneath
a hearthstone. I suppose such trust and faith in her
alleged lover as has been displayed by the young lady
from Bathurst are Beautiful and Womanly Attributes,
but they are liable to make her relatives bald with worry,
if she doesn’t trade some of them away for a little horse-
sense. She—in the universal meaning of the word—
never will, though. Women with what is called the
connubial temperament would—like Goethe’s Martha—
swear everlasting devotibn to Satan if he sued to them
in tan boots and a coat that didn’t wrinkle at the
shoulders

a row, you know.”
When the matric. exam, is under way (doesn’t it seem
cruel to time it for hot and muggy March, when every-
body is at mental ebb-tide?), a posse of she-dragons are
hired to keep an eagle eye on the girl candidates, to see
that they don’t produce pirated answers out of their
coiffures, or set down book-worked mathematics, carefully
pencilled beforehand on their shirt-cuffs. The dragons
are supposed to be here, there and everywhere, and hand'
glasses of water to fainting students, who are not allowed
free access to the filter, lest they should stuff some con-
traband tips up the tap for a chum “in the know” to
profit by, and the literary duenna of the ’Varsity who
does her duty must be very brisk about it. But one
dragon at the recent seance did most of her “beat”
sitting down, and only kept her sleepless eye on the
victims within range, so any girl in the back, who had
risked it to stuff her hollow tooth with useful data, had
lovely opportunities. By the way, I hear that prigging
was largely indulged in by the girls’ division, and that
when a subject rises and floors a student individually, she
simply keeps her side-eye on the nearest girl who is
wrestling with it.
Which all reminds me that the whole governing body,
male and female, of the Sydney Women’s College means to
rise and give Miss Jessie Macdonald the brand-new lady-
principal of the Women’s part a reception in the great
ball-room, soon after her arrival. No date has been given
so far. N.B.—No matter how 7 “intellectual” and ad-
vanced the lady it seems always necessary 7 for her to be
brought to her bearings with tea and cake.
Cuttica dressed Traviata very well, but she looked her
best in the cream robe-de-chambre of the death-scene,
where she ought to (between ourselves) manage her
skirts a good deal better. Signora Tresani wore an
artistic dress, cut low enough to reveal a dimpled pair of
shoulders, in the Empire style—black lace en trainci
bordered with crushed pink roses, and looped on the left
over pink silk; but Sisco in a towzled wig, as if he had
pelted through a rose-bush, might have sat for a photo,
of the bogey man.

which refuses to assist beggarly old invalid ladies,

may I he permitted to enquire whether lady Samaritans
are so scarce that it is necessary to thrust a man into
the privacy 7 of female indigence, in order to discover the

The revival of “The Arabian Nights” at Sydney
Criterion served to show off two pretty 7 toilets at any
rate, I mean Marie Fraser’s electric-blue silk, long-
tailed tea-gown with dove front of soft white-striped stuff
bound across Bernhardtishly with silver lace ; and child-
voiced Ida Liston’s accordeon-pleated canary-silk skirt
and bodice with corselet of brown velvet and wondrous
sleeves-flat hot trimmed with yellow roses and brown
velvet vulgarising the pretty frock down to the expected
sartorial level of the India-rubber girl—for nobody short
of a variety artist off the chain would make street wear of
so festive a garment. But what I want to ask is—“Do
music-hall darlings always sit on the table swinging then-
feet and smoking cigarettes to the long wash of brandy-
and-soda ? Is there really no other phase of the song-and-
dance lady in her unguarded moments?” In the first
production of the comedy under discussion Maggie Knight
sat on the table and smoked, and Ida Liston casually
comes along four years afterwards and sits on the table
and smokes, and you begin to have a dazed suspicion that
the red-hot toot of the average Tottie must become dis-
mally monotonous in the fourth decade. Or is it that
your skirted Thcspienne doing music-hall business in a
drawing-room comedy clings to the wayworn stage direc-
tions because she is so many miles above evolving new
and hilarious business out cf her ow n innerconsciousness?
Her narrow notion of wliat constitutes untrammelled
behaviour is on all-fours with the laborious way in which
she makes believe to get through a stage-direction
cigarette, which, in private, she would probably devour
in two gasps. By the way, it looks most unnatural to see
an actress shying at cigarettes-as a rule they have a
genuine mania for paper puffs. (I suppose the wicked
editor will delete my italics.)
One tiling is positive-patrons of Sydney Koyal are cer-
tainly cooler at Bland Holt’s plays than they were at the
opera, for, no matter how full the house, those melodious
exiles wouldn’t have the root opened one inch, lest “ye
damp air of Sydenia ” would foggify their treble registers,
and wasn’t it hot in consequence! There is period
staging and dressing all through a “Sailor’s Knot”
(action in the reign of George IV.); the bygone garbings
of the Regency were correctly done and made up intogay
and novel stage pictures from post to finish. Mrs. Bland
Holt (she got a hearty welcome and complimentary
flowers, by the way) looked well in her high-waisted blue
gown with white muslin apron and fichu, and Regency
bonnet (they are going to be fashionable again, the
milliners tell me) in white straw and blue ribbons and
feathers worn in the first act; but the quaint yellow silk
gown with soft white muslin kerchief and poke bonnet in
yellow straw and ribbons, which she wore in the wedding
scene, was simply scrumptious in effect. Kate Bishop was
cordially supported by her wardrobe also, and sported a
pale-pink silk, a white silk with fichu of orange-blossoms
and curtained bonnet en suite, and a white brocade with
flashing diamonds, which best became her. By the way,
her gowns were snipped with rather an exceeding snip in
front—but I suppose that was only severe chronological
correctness, for don’t you always feel a mad longing to
interpolate a deep lace tucker into the oil-painting of
your grandmother?
Mrs. Bernard-Bcerc, who arrived at Melbourne the
other day, and was “received” on board the specially-
chartered pleasure-steamer Ozone as she stepped from the
R.M.S. Orient, is described for me by an esteemed corres-
pondent ns “ a cross between Mrs. Brown-Potter and the
Bernhardt, but without the beauty of Kyrlo Bellew's
admirer or the sprightliness of the divine Sara.” Mrs.
Beercwore opal-tinted shot silk perfectly plain as to the
skirt and a handsome seal mantle w ith sable cuffs and
collar, a black feather boa and a ravishing little black hat
w ith a knot of seagreen velvet, and a spotter! veil. The long
lady, who has rather small, scintillating grey eyes and a
rather small nose, owns a generous but pleasant mouth,
smiles frequently, and shows a splendid set of teeth. She
was very pale and was suffering from the effects of
influenza. In his speech of welcome the ever-beautiful
and juvenile J. ,T. Butters went back into the genesis
of Australian threatrical history, beginning with
Brooke and ending with Bcrnard-Beere, and assur-
ing that lady that all the stars who came to Australia
were B.'s to a man and woman. Then someone solemnly
said, as a compliment, that the fame of M. Marius hid
preceded him, and everyone winked at his neighbour.
Mrs. Bernard-Be ere, in a rich thrilling voice thanked
everyone for their good wishes, and said she hoped to be
able to act up to her initials. St. John Marius is a dapper
little man with an nggressively-elippui moustache and a
remarkably long chin. A dudish-looking youth carried
round a box which everyone supposed to contain 8.-B.’s
jewels. Herbert Standing is a jolly-looking man, and

tctors wore check suits; they were all in black coats.
Eccles Anson presented everyone to Mrs. 8.-8., and
Charles Ryley, who brought his patent double-action sneer
with him, supported Eccles, who looked glad when the
business was over. One of the ladies of the company (an
American) wore a rough tweed tailor-gown that was per-
fectly cut and fitted to perfection, and although the lady
had no collar nor brooch and only a small felt hat with a
brown veil, she fairly took the cake for good style. It
was a splendid idea to take Mrs. Bernard-Beere across to
St. Kilda and drive her to the Esplanade Hotel, where she
will occupy the suite used by Bernhardt. Think of the
difference to a stranger who first sees Melbourne via Port
Melbourne and its vile backyards ! P.M. is seemingly
composed of back-yards where it is always washing-day.
Just at present people who dine in restaurants should
shun tempting dishes served with mushroom trimmings,
because those damp and dangerous weeds are coming in
mingled with the suicidal toadstool, and the mushroom"
gatherers who supply the city market root up the just
and the unjust in ecpial quantities, while the undertaker
smiles joyously on their unprejudiced industry. Only
the other day in a Sydney cafe a young man ate some
steak and alleged mushrooms for lunch. Tw’enty minutes
after he was flat on his back in a drug-shop with an
excited chemist pouring draughts into him by the jugful,
while an assistant went for a doctor and the police.
Heroic remedies at last prevailed, and that young man
may now go and revel in mushrooms for the rest of
his life, for few people get poisoned twice accidentally.
I have received two “Australian National” songs—
“ Federation ; or the Future Republic,” dedicated to Mr.
“Toby” Barton by the composers, S. Mackay and Edgar
Winson, and “The Flag of the South,” music by L. J.
Bogle, and words by E. J. Brady. The first-named is a
sort of catalogue in G major, setting forth just what
furniture Australia wants to set up as a Free State with,
and scrupulously set to a tune which would be the very
thing for —a children’s evening hymn. The second
warble—well, I do detest over-earnestness, which means
bathos. How feeble are all the modern “cant” denun-
ciations of tyrants, and so on, when you know that
they have been translated into ineffectual English from
the French of the Revolutionary time 1 My opinion of
the Australian is that he won’t sing patriotic songs—save
with his tongue in his cheek. He will not destroy tyrants
with froth, and what I have heard called “ blather.” He
will approach them and lay them low in a spirit, not of
guileless enthusiasm, but of cold-blooded, calculating
cynicism.
The spectacle that riveted one’s gaze in the trial trip of
Seville’s Barber, at the Royal, the other evening, was
Sisco’s shanks, and if their beautiful curves were only
cotton-wadding I certainly advise spider-finished members
of the ballet to go on their knees to him for the recipe.
The women, by the way, are all in love with Sisco’s hands,
small and fine, with finger-tips taper as a terrier’s tail
before the dog-fancier’s teeth meet through it—but me-
thinks the blunt-fingered and ham-shaped masculine paw
has more lasting attractions for my gushful sex when its
head is cool —it really doesn’t do for a lover to clasp his
darling’s fists with a shapely hand a size daintier than
your own, you know. Mrs. Cuttica was the Rosina, and
sported a fancy frock of white brocade, short skirt and
bodice with Vandyke of black ; Figaro bodice in maroon
velvet with long sleeves, and trimmed with gold bullion
fringe, crimson roses in her hair and crimson fan—a turn-
out so chipper in effect that, I will venture to bet, a re-re-
re-duplicated Rosina will stalk at lots of costume balls
next season. But isn't it interesting to note how the
tenor never will appear en deshabille ? Signor C., when
disguised as the substitute for a seedy professor of music,
wore his hat three sizes less exaggerated than Travaglini s,
and his spruce black gaberdine was relieved with a point-
lace cravat—while Signor C. T. got up a good comedy
effect in rusty robes and a hat resembling an infant’s
coffin. Tenori, as a matter of useful fact, always like to
be taken seriously and sentimentally, and they resent
whimsical make-up almost as deeply as the painted
soprano of a comic opera Co. loathes and disparages the
convulsing by-play of the funny-man when she is warbling
in a high-class manner “ down centre.”
The circle was a gay spectacle, and many “ thoughtful”
costumes were there to do the Barber reverence. Mrs.
S. A. Moss, in heliotrope-silk net, relieved with spring
green-diamonds, twinkling here and there, had filled her
box with friends. Carrie Swain, in black tulle and silk
and diamonds. A snappy dress was of amber bengaline,
with high Zouave collar embroidered in gold beads and

robe, and donned in compliment to Sisco. Speaking
about opera audiences, isn’t it odd that Lady J., who is
supposed to surge in the van of intellect and culchaw,
never once went to the Maccaroni show at the Royal?
Not, mind you, that the troupe felt a sinking sen-
sation because she didn’t accord her socially mag-
netic patronage—for the season was a great success—
but allee same, it is usual for the vice regal people to lend
rapt countenances to all high-class exhibitions that have
an educative tendency, even if the said vice regal party
are cruelly bored at the whole thing, and are hungering
and thirsting for a curtained box and something with
tights in it.

Talking about teeth, a fond Sydney parent was question-
ing his little daughter on what she had learnt that day at
school. “ ’Bout teeth,” chirped the cherub. Then Pa.
waded in to show his grasp of the subject: “Well; what
arc the first teeth!” “Incisors and molars,” answered
she. “And what are the last?” “ False teeth.”

The last week of Grand Lallan Opera at
Sydney Royal was a busy one. “Faust,” “Un
Ballo in Masohera,” and “II Trovatore” were
successively presented, and the most delightful
effort, of Rossini’s genius, “II Barbiere di
Siviglia ” was produced with genuine and well-
nigh absolutely triumphant success. The Alma-
viva of the recent operatic essay was Signor
Cuttica, who laboured - energetically and con-
scientiously against vocal disadvantages which
proved almost too much of a handicap for his
undoubtedly clever acting, and which nearly
stranded him once or twice upon the musical shale
and shingle created by the composer for the tenor
role. The well-mannered and sumptuously-attiied
Figaro was a thing of beauty and baritone melli-
fluousness in the hands of Signor Sisco, and the
vocal profundity of Signor Travaglini surrendered
glimpses of its unfathomable gloom in the per-
sonality of bis grimly humorous conception of the
character of Don Basilic. The opera, however, is
really the propeity of Don Bartolo and his clever,
saucy, and interesting ward, Rosina. As the
love-sick and jealous old beau, Signor lorio was
simply delightful. He revelled and rioted in the
humour of the part, and kept the house in a gentle
simmer of amusement with quaint interpolations
of broken English. Signora Cuttica was the
Rosina of the production, and her delicate and
artistic singing, and her piquant acting will com-
bine to render her characterisation of this part a
melodious memory when Italy shall be annexed by
Malta, and opera shall be no more. During the
singing lesson in the third act she sang on separate
occasions two valses, “Vieni” and “Danziamo
Voliamo,” composed by Signor Maffezzoli, the
conductor, who, we are told, has already b Q d an
opera, “Raffaelo,” produced at Milan.

At Sydney Royal, on Saturday night last,
the decks were cleared for action, and the British
tars of an olden period boarded the vocal groves
erstwhile sacred to the 'iuscan warbler. The
house was packed from floor to ceiling.
“A Sailor’s Knot” proved itself a huge melo-
dramatic success, and ought to have a consider-
able grip of popular favour before it is untied.
The plot is involved and complicated with distinct
obligations to Douglas Jerrold and “ Black-eyed
Susan,” and the dialogue is indebted in several
places to Josh Billings and other humourists. The
scene of action wanders from Wapping Old Staiis
to Greenwich Hospital, from Greenwich Hospital
to Stepney Old Church, from this ecclesiastical
antiquity to an old-time three-decker, from the
ship to a French chateau, and finally finds a rest-
ing-place at military head-quarters, on the road to
Paris, subsequent to the Battle of Waterloo. The
period is the year 1814, or thereabouts, and tlie
air is filled with Dibdin’s music, and pig-tai!s
float upon the ocean breeze. Two brothers of
more than human virtue, in love with a French
lady who has a fatal inclination to marriage with
whomsoever she happens to meet last, annex
all the spare sympathy of the audience, and two
villains of unspeakable depravity occupy their
business hours and their leisure moments with
throwing obstacles in the path of virtue, and are
rewarded witli the genuine disapprobation of the
gallery. Mr. Charles Glenny undertakes one of
the virtuous brothers and Mr. Walter Howe the
other. The former is the strong man of the show
and a genuine actor of considerable tragic power.
Miss Kate Bishop supports the weakness of
character assigned to the French lady with
the easy propensity to matrimonial contracts,
and does it surprisingly well. Mr. W. E.
Baker is one of the villains, a polished and
Mephistophelean disciple of Voltaire, with a
refined acquaintance with the paths of crooked-
ness, and Mr. Albert Norman is the Biitish
scoundrel, with a crabbed character and an un-
kempt head of hair. The comedy is supplied by
Mr. Bland Holt—as a jolly young waterman,
with a costume which is a cross between that of a
postman and that of a jockey—and Mrs. Holt, as
his sweetheart, and this lady is as pretty and
piquant a damsel as any man might reasonably
desire to fall in love with. There are several
hundred other characters in this nautical effort of
Mr. Pettitt’s—which is divided into four decks —
and many yards of delectably-painted canvas from
the brushes of Messrs. George and J. Gordon
and H. Grist.
** * *
Sydney Her Majesty’s is at length relieved
from the baleful presence of the Prince of Dark-
ness, and Messrs. Sims and Pettitt’s pomp and
circumstance, entitled “In the Ranks,” is suffer-
ing a brief revival, in order to enable the average
peaceable citizen an opportunity of contemplating
at his well-fed ease the joys of barrack life. This
drama simply wallows in crimson uniforms, and
revels in red-ochre. Geo. Ripnold Henry V.
suffers meritoriously, and everybody is liappy
ever afterwards. As Ned Drayton, we love
him more enthusiastically than as Mephisto. He
is fat, fair, and forty—and all the ladies love
him. Miss Watts Phillips assists the hero through
a catalogue of woe, and shares with him the
delectation of unalloyed bliss represented by
several thousands per annum. Mr. Geo. P, Carey
is the Colonel Wyuter of the revival, and lie dis-
penses cheap morality of the G. R. Sims brand at
reasonable and seductive rates. For the rest,
much is as it used to be, with Mr. Hazlitt as an
unadulterated villain, and Mr. Jewett as a vir-
tuous captain of light infantry. Mr, J. W.
Sweeney’s Corporal O’Leary is likewise a clever
performance, and Mr. Joe Tolano is too good for
this world, and heaven is his home.

A supplementary season of grand Italian
opera opened at Sydney Garrick, on Saturday
last, with Verdi’s tragic and romantic opera of
“ La Traviata,” the libretto of which is founded
on the story of “ La Dame aux Camelias,” by the
younger Dumas. On Monday, again, notwith-
stantfing wet weather, the house was crowded.
Signora Cuttica was the Violetta of the pre-
sent cast ; Signor Cuttica was Alfredo, and
Signor Sisco, Germont, Alfredo’s father. From
a musical point of view the production was a com-
plete and unqualified success, but it was poorly
upholstered, and the characters were eccentrically
attired. Germont appeared as a faded and
rakish-looking cavalier of the court of Charles 1.,
with flowing looks and looped and plumed beaver;
while Alfredo, with a closely-cropped head,
fluctuated between whiles from the 10th to the 18th
century, and Violetta never got away from the
nineteenth —as far as costume was concerned. In
the matter of furniture the management indulged
its eccentricities to the utmost. The dying scene
was only saved from being ridiculous by the
superb singing of Signora Cuttica, for there, in
the middle of the sick room, stood a withered
copy of Charles 1., confronted by an iron bedstead
of the aggressively modern brand, and flanked on
the one hand by a Chinese-made chest of drawers,
and on the other by a Chinese made dressing-
tab’e. For all the world it looked like a dissipated
elderly gentleman returning from a fancy-dress
ball to tin! his wife sitting up for him. Apart
from these irritating incongruities this production
of “Traviata” was highly satisfactory, and
Signora Cuttica in the title rdle excelled all her
previous performances in Sydney, “Lucia,” per-
haps, excepted. It is certainly a long time since
we have heard anything like the singing by this
gifted lady of the final scene of the first act.
Apart from his ridiculous make-up, Signor Sisco
was an admirable Germont, and Signor Cuttica
was better as Alfredo than as anything else he has
played in since Elgardo, in “ Lucia.” On Tuesday
“Lucrezia Borgia” was produced, with Signonna
Guidotti in the name-part of Donizetti’s master-
piece ; and on Friday Flotow’s five-act comedy
opera of “Martha” revived historical recollec-
tions of Queen Anne and the old-time customs of
our great-grandfathers.
* * * *
The Polytechnic has recently indulged in
marionettes, Wild West wonders, and a gas
explosion, which last contributed a great deal to
the advertisement of this delectable catacomb.
The Coogee and other Aquariums continue in the
marine enjoyment of much floating patronage,
and the Cyclorama still lights the way to a
patriotic death. At the Garrick every Sunday
evening the voice of the warbler is heard in the
land, and Mr. Larmour has arranged to take the
Criterion every week on the same day for the
same purpose.

Dan Barry’s Dramatic Company opened
at Victoria Theatre, Newcastle, N.S.W., on
Monday, in “The Parson’s Oath,” described as a
thrilling, realistic and humorous piece from the pen
of the late Spurgeon, and dealing with the lights
and shades of London life. Dan makes a unique
“clerk in holy orders,” as Rev. John Audley, and
is well supported by a large and efficient
company. sv

“Randolph the Reckless” was let 1005. 6
at Melbourne Royal on Saturday before a crowd
of almost Boxing Night dimensions. Officially
described as an extravaganza, “ Randolph ” is in
most places, a compound of old and new style
burlesque, sufficiently ridiculous to be funny. But
at times the scene is reduced to a cheerless corri-
dor, whither each leading performer wanders in
turn to do nothing in particular, beyond diverting
attention from the clamour of carpenters in the
rear. On these occasions, “ Randolph ” resembles
the stop-gap portions of a pantomime, and ceases
to be reckless. If the dialogue were smarter, the
music more original, the orchestra and chorus
stronger, and a couple of the principal characters
less obtrusive, one might have great hopes of the
present entertainment. As things went on
Saturday, the general effect was rather thin, and
when the audience saw the end approaching,
they cleared out with more haste than is cus-
tomary, even in Australia. However, the success
or failure of a gaudy leg-show of this class
is not necessarily decided by a first-night verdict.
“Randolph” is not trammelled by a plot, and
may be turned into something quite different
without the least prejudice to the author’s idea,
because ho never had an idea to start with.
Brim ton has painted some capital scenery for the
production, and when the big stage isn’t shut out
from view by the aforesaid corridor cloth the
popular demand for splendour is fully complied
with. Billee Barlow, in the name part, is more
Farrenish than usual, and remains on terms of
strong personal friendship with her “house ” all
the time. Her appearance is a host of talent in
itself, in addition to which she sings several songs
in best music-hall style, and woos the heroine
with great gus:o Her excuse for not kneeling to
make love—viz., that it would spoil her tights—is
amply sufficient. They certainly are beyootiful
tights. Rumour alleges that a fairy wove thehs
on Billee when she was a baby, since which time
she has expanded within their silken toils until
they have become incorporated with her very
being. Walton, as the villain of the piece, is
droll and nimble, also Harry Shine acts up to
his name. Edwin Kelly, Lillian Davies, and
Harry Daniels make a good impression, and an
interpolated performance by the Tokio Troupe of
Jap. jugglers gives pleasure to all. But Edith
Vane and Harry Barnes are drawbacks. The
lady is fairly vivacious and happy every now and
then, but as a ballad-singer she requires judicious
curtailment, and will be better appreciated when
she remains silent. Her songs don’t reach the
heart, somehow. They pierce the ear and attack
the liver, but pass the susceptible bnzzum coldly
by. As for Harry Barnes, in short skirts and a
sham nose, he must moderate his transports forth-
with. The programme states that Barnes has
played his character for two years in England,
but this proves nothing except that the British
race is becoming dotty in its old age. Mr, B. is
now in the kawlinies, a stranger in a strange land,
and if he likes to make a point of always blotting
himself out in the first act, his early disappear-
ance will bo thankfully received.

** * *
“ Captain Swift” is given a reasonable
chance to show his quality at Melbourne Bijou.
On Saturday night the story of the retired bush-
ranger, who shoots himself to spare his mother’s
feelings and oblige her family, was greeted with
great gusts of applause at the end of every act and
important exit. The breathless silence that
reigned around the most pathetic incidents was
broken only by the splash of falling tears, and
when the house was required to feel a thrill, it
experienced that sensation in all its phases.
Charles Warner used to overact the gallant
Captain throughout. He put too much bright
paint on the character, and gurgled in his agony
like the last quart of water that is escaping from
a bath. Nor was his general support strong
enough for the play. The B. and B. rendering of
Haddon Chambers’ tricky work is, on the whole,
more human and impressive. Titheradgotones the
melodramatic adventurer down without sacrificing
an iota of effect, and manages to make strong
points without calling attention to the manufac-
ture of them. He doesn’t ring a muffin-bell of
by play, so to speak, every time he is going to be
startled, or horror-stricken, but he keeps the public
eye fixed upon his every movement, and that
potential optic takes in the situation without
blinking. The author represents Captain Swift
as a clever impostor, bold and resourceful, who
would certainly have “ taken in ” a quiet family
but for the presence amongst them of a squatter
he had stuck up in days of yore, and of a degraded
menial in black, who fills the position of resident
undertaker to the family and is always taking
mental measurements for everybody’s coffin.
Titheradge realises the conception of the hero,
whereas Warner obtruded his false pretences, and
went out of his way to create suspicion, like the
gentleman who planted his young bride under the
hearthstone. This change of treatment accounts, in
the first place, for the reception given on Saturday
to a play that created little enthusiasm three years
ago. Mrs. Brough, in the part of next impor-
tance, is very good—considering. She is not a
great emotional actress, and, if she were, the
character would still be many years too old for
her. Boucicault may pass with honours, for the
elderly husband, but Mrs. Brough will never
satisfy the world that she is Tith.’s ma. In
fact, no young woman can convincingly add a day
to her age in comedy or drama, although many
elderly charmers can knock off a quarter of a
century without outraging probability. Mrs.
Brough, notwithstanding her youngness in years
and manner, is a pleasant surprise in this arduous
part, but that’s about all. A negative success in-
stead of being a disastrous failure. Her predecessor
was a disastrous failure. Sass is smart and manly
as the squattah, and Lawrence very picturesque
and striking as the resident suggestion of the
family vault. Emma Temple plays the “Cap-
tain’s ” mash prettily, and artists like Cecil Ward,
Pattie Browne, Corlesse, and Emma Gwynne in
minor parts naturally tend to make them seem
bigger. “ Captain Swift ” deserves to capture the
town.

The variety show at Melbourne Opera
House finishes to-night (Thursday) with a benefit
to the Craggs. On Saturday the accomplished
Mrs. Bernard-Beere will flash her genius at this

* * iff *
Dampier has seized a great opportunity
by the throat and means to shake it for all it is
worth. On Saturday next (19th.) the stage of
Melbourne Alexandra will be dedicated to “Wilful
Murder,” a new realistic drama of powerful local
interest. The villain intends to bury his victim
up the chimney, or somewhere, and the horrid
deed will be discovered by its overwhelming smell,
according to all accounts. Melbourne is ripe for
this drama, and Dampier can be congratulated
upon a solid business success even before the
corpse has been discovered. The name of the
author of “Wilful Murder” is being kept dark
for the present, but the police have a clue to his
identity, and the truth must soon leak out. The
play was really written by the Divine Wi'liams.

At Poverty Point.
When “Our Boys” was first read at London Vaude-
ville, the assemblage included D. James, T. Thorne,
Warner, and Barren, on the look-out for their parts, and
all agreed that the play was “ weak, very weak.” At the
last rehearsal, all agreed again that it would run only a
month, but the run was four years. When Buckstone, at
the llaymarket, produced a play called “Mont Blanc,”
the performers could not rehearse for laughing. Not a
laugh at night, and this piece ruined Bucky.
