It was done in Melbourne by Brooke —Brooke as the
Duke, Edwards as Angelo, Mrs. Heir as Isabella.
Johnny Hall used to tell how an opera by Lecoccj was
manufactured, in London, at the Comique, named the
“Black Prince,” wherein he acted Dr. Maresnest. They
bought up a heap of Lecocq’s early fragments of music,
and Welded them into “a new opera by Lccocq,” who
wrote a bitter letter to the English press.
One of the race of Yankee showmen who visit Australia
to teach the natives how to ride bucking horses, and who
thereupon appear in a circus mounted on an exhausted
cab-moke and wonder why the local inhabitant doesn’t
applaud, was sentenced the other day at Murrurundi
(N.S.W.) to six years confinement for discrepancies in the
ownership of a horse. About an hour after the sentence
was passed there was a missing cowboy in Murrurundi
gaol. The long-haired one had fled over the wall.

Some excursionists being wrecked off Green Island,
near Cairms (N.Q.), got ashore in a very dilapidated con-
dition, and started for Cairns veritable tatterdemalions.
On the track they met a travelling circus, the proprietor
of which genially offered them an engagement to appear
as a band of Sioux Indians, with a special inducement to
one hairy wreck to pose as “ the Wild Man of the \V oods,
lassoocd in the Rocky Mountains, after killing 17 of his
capturers.” They didn't deal.
Wills, the dramatist, had always some ungrateful
pensioner on hand. He picked a broken-down, starving
University man out of the gutter once, and look him
home, and appointed him his secretary. He didn’t want
a secretary, but all the same lie paid the ungrateful and
unlovely old wreck’s salary till the day of his death. The
Secretary was always coughing, snuffling, wheezing and
complaining; he never did any work and he groaned all
day long. One winter night some friends called on Wills
and found him smoking his pipe on the pavement and
gambolling about to keep from freezing. The old wreck
had issued his ukase that tobacco made him feel worse,
and Wills took his pipe outside rather than injure the
venerable one’s feelings by reminding him that he didn’t
own the premises.

It was inevitable that the more candid of the London
critics would complain that Nellie Stewart overacted.
The fault has been growing on Nellie ever since her first
visit to England, where, by the way, she improved the
power and range of her voice at the expense of its music.
At latest dates it was expected that Johnny Toole
would soon be well enough to appear in a new farcical
comedy. Better that the little man should live for ever
in a harmless, happy state of convalesence—strong enough
to enjoy himself but not sufficiently robust to go on
acting.

The plot consists of Death coming in from time to time

family. That’s all.

A Melbourne paper mentions that “Professor Archibald
is delighting large audiences in Calcutta with his phono-
graph.” Archibald, by the way, stands in much the same
relation to the Edison instrument ns does an Italian
organ-grinder to his portable box of music. Either pro-
fessor turns a handle and the sound comes out. In fact,
it is hard to see what particular wisdom and learning the
phonograph attendant makes profession of. The little
apparatus hadn’t been long at Melbourne Waxworks
before Phil Stuart could work it just as well as Archibald,
who always kept up a pretence of being the only man in
the know.

Antony, interspersed with biblical references to the rout
of the Midianites by wandering Israel, and the fall of
Jericho. The last item finished it.

The Australian Theatrical Management Company is
going into peaceful liquidation. In the present state of
the market there is no hope for the shareholders to
recover the money dropped over Laura Villiers. Coppin
is right. “Things have been done too well here. They
have now reached such a standard that unless you keep
up to it you do no business, and if you do keep up to it
you will lose money. That is exactly the position of
theatrical matters now.” The Bulletin put it in rhyme
a few months back. Wise Garner to clear out.
A circus-manager was hanged at Greenock the other
week, unnoticed by the cable-man—though, perhaps, this
was the criminal over whose doctor-bungled execution
man-butcher Berry resigned. The culprit stabbed his
leading equestrienne through the heart, because she
couldn’t satisfactorily answer the question why she had
“treated him like a dog,” by declining to be mashed.
Circuses pride themselves on their high moral tone, by
the way. The ladies are mostly married to members of
the company they travel with, and monogamy is almost a
masculine rule of the perfesh.

a well-known figure in Melbourne has just been
removed. This was a young man with only one arm and
one leg, who possessed remarkable powers as a whistler,
He never asked anyone for help; he would walk up the
st reet on his crutches, select his pitch, and commence
“I Dreamt.” After giving a couple of selections, he
would move on to another spot, without passing round
the hat. If anyone felt charitably disposed, they walked
up while he was whistling and put their contribution into
his hand ; he would put it into his pocket without look-
ing at it, or thanking the donor. The poor whistler has
been arrested as a vagrant,

Wadham Wyndham, who slew his wife
and family and is now confined in Darlinghurst
Prison, is a remarkably well-read man, but has an
inveterate prejudice against Dickens. This is
solely on account of a passacre in the opening
chapter of “David Copperfield,” in which the
debut of the hero on life’s stage is stated to be
“ proticipated ” (as Mrs. Gamp would say) by
Miss Betsy Trotwood appearing at the parlour
window and pressing her nose flat against the
window-pane. Wyndham considers the introduc-
tion of the incident grossly indelicate, and will not
read a page of the great novelist’s other works,
although he admits that he may be hypersensitive.
The unfortunate man is a voracious reader of
fiction notwithstanding, and worries through
every book that passes the learned scrutiny of
Canon Rich. One day he consulted a late arrival
as to anything fresh in ephemeral literature, and
was told of a work by Frank Barrett, entitled
“ Fettered for Life.” His eye glistened, and he said
ho would requisition. It may be mentioned
that as W. is confined during “the Queen's
p'easuro ” he is allowed exceptional indulgences,
he has Ids cell half lined, is allowed to send for
approved books, and woiks only by his own
choice; in addition to which he is permitted
the en oyment of gas till fl p in. Ilis informant
warned him that the book had a yellow covei wit h
the representation of two convicts going to work
at Portland under the supervision of an armed
warder, and that it would be unlikely to “pass.
The vaticination was correct enough.
Scientific Rabbiting.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
You know Bacchus Marsh. That was the locality,
and the town was red with rabbiters. My busi-
ness was to collect and book the animals for the
vans that came twice a week. Friday was our
Sunday, because carcases of that day’s manu-
facture would not sell the next day but one.
Therefore, on Friday the men went into the Marsh
“to buy a few things.” But an enterprising
Frenchman called De Boosie brought the things
within nearer range, and with Madame and wine
secured the patronage of six of my best trappers.
It is of a party of three that I am going to tell.
They were all from the South of Scotland some-
where about the Annan, and had probably been
poachers at home, and they made big money, paid
in cash fortnightly. I was camped on a sandhill
half a mile nearer to the road than they were, and
used to ferret and shoot so as to pass the time.
One Friday I was in some bushes close to the
tent, when De Boosie drove up in his spring-cart.
He had apparently no load, but got down and
looked into my tent. I never liked the man, and
loved him less after I saw what he did. Ho had
made sure that I was from home, and nowhere
handy.
As I remarked, I lived among sand. Then he
drew his mare in front of my camp, unloosed the
belly-band and raised the point of the shafts.
First of all, he had unpinned the tail-board.
With a crash of breaking glass, five bags (ap-
parently) were launched on the soft ground. The
tail-board was adjusted, the belly-band buckled,
and the frog-eater was mounting, when I showed
up. There were poor, old Geordy Halliday, Billy
Grearson and Tommy Dumfries, spread out “quite
loose on the strand, and a peaceful-like smile on
their features,” and the sand red with the blood
of bottles.
That Frenchman never brought me another load
of wine and rabbiters. The =£2 fine for aggra-
vated assault was paid by his victims. The police
hunted Freuchy to some other rabbiting camps,
and he died as he lived—a dog with a taint on
his soul. But the scientific part—that consisted
in the skilful manner in which the three Scotch-
men slittered (that’s the word) over the sand,
with one of the unbroken bottles in each hand.
They were too drunk to walk ; Wully went on
hands and knees (the bottles were like snow-shoes
for him); Tarn used the bottles as pegs, planting
the thin ends and then drawing himself up to
them ; but, Georgy, poor old Halliday ! lie rolled
to the shade of a tree.
Where are they now ? Grearson’s buried on a
station; Dumfries is married and unhappy, whilst
the rolling-stone is paralysed in one of the great
asylums of Sydney. Better be like Grearson.
“•There were giants in those days.”

The policy of the really democratic party
all over Australia is to keep all railways in the
hands of the State. If Minister Lyne, (N.S.W.),
who is wobbling on the subject, pastes this in bis
hat, it will save him a heap of trouble.

The peascod Patriotic League is at logger-
hea ls with the Australian Natives’ Association,
in Melbourne. One of the chief Patriotic fugle-

to denounce Royalty before he was shifted one of
the A.N.A.
Labour-member Ryan, who now represents
the Barcoo (Q.), was one of the alleged “riotous
conspirators ” arrested at Barcaldino last year,
and tried at Rockhampton. He was acquitted.
Now, lie represents Barcaldine in the Bananaland
Parliament.
SA. “ National Defence League ” is
working well! Recently the Adelaide Licensing
Bench refused to grant a club license to the
Adelaide Democratic Club, which has been
extremely aggressive (and successful) politically
during the last four years.
According to Reynolds’ Newspaper,
there is still on the British Statute Book a law,
passed in the reign of Henry V., but never acted
upon since the time of Charles IT., prescribing
payment of members, at a rate which would now
be equivalent to 255. a day.
A suggestion is made in England that the
demand for land for small holdings might be met
by permitting land-owners to pay succession-
duties in land, such land to become irrevocably
the property of the State, and to be leased in
perpetuity to persons of the agricultural labour
class.
The worm has turned, 3000 strong. At
“General” Booth’s last great meeting of the
“ submerged tenth ” the audience howled down
their benefactor as a “sweater” and a fraud, and
plainly intimated their belief that he was making
money out of their distresses. The big drum will
revolt next.
It is stated, on the pious authority
Robert Harper, M.L.A., that the one great pur-
pose of the Young Victorian Patriotic League is
“to defend their country, to fight for it, aye, and
to die for it,” in the cause of Property against
democracy. A political association for firing low
and laying ’em out, seemingly.
Premier Playford, in his private letters
from India, says that “ Tamil” (i.c., cheap nigger)
labour would be just suitable for the Northern
Territory. This confirms recent Bulletin warn-
ings. Yet Inspector Parkes, the S.A. Govern-
ment’s own officer, reports that white labour is
most fitted for the N.T.
Lord Masham, who was one of Gobden’s
Freetrade chairmen in ’47, at a recent Empire
Trade League conference to advocate preferential
tariffs between Britain and the colonies :—“ Were
Cobden now alive, ho (C.) would advocate the
present professed principlesof the League.” Which
are nothing if not Protectionist!
The Victorian Minister of Customs meekly
apologises for having issued cheap licenses to
manufacture cigars, whereby the “representative”
weed factories find a portion of their trade diverted
into humble channels. In granting the concession,
says Minister Turner, “he had no intention of
starting small manufacturers to compete with the
large establishments!”
The Argus, speaking on the coming
election, says that on one side (the One-man-one-
yote) are arrayed the criminals, loafers, and float-
ing population of labourers, while on the other
(the Dual-vote side) is the thrifty, industrious,
and honest section of the community. What a
crime it is to be poor ! Workmen of Victoria,
remember the Berry time and accept this the
brutal challenge of your traditional enemy.
If the Saviour re-appeared on earth in
Australia, he would be set down as one of the
“unthrifty” class, and, as a nomad, would have
no vote at all, while Balfour, Meudell, Jerry
Rundle, “ Cokum ” Epps, Murray-Prior, Tommy
Kelly—all good Christians, mind you—would have
at least two apiece ! “ Give all your goods to feed
the poor !” There— be it reverently said—is a
picture for you : the landless pauper, Jesus, before
the Australian bogus-National Association.

is especially eager for the coming fray in the

“ fighting anarchy.” In the event of no anarchy
coming forward to get fits, the truculent ram-

supporters of law and order,

Last week the first GO of Griffilwraith’s
escaped cheap Italians struck Charters Towers
(Q.), arid found work at cutting timber for use in
the mines. That is to say, they offered to cut the
timber at a vastly-reduced rate,' and GO Australians
were promptly sacked to make room for them.
Nearly every Italian has now deserted the plan-
tations, and the Government, rather than acknow-
ledge the awful smash of its project, is supplying
them with rations and free railway passes to go
south or west or anywhere. Tin’s sort of thing,
with the Kanaka and the land-grant railway
syndicate swindle thrown in, is the beginning of
the end.

The unemployed who got work on the new
Donald (Vic.), railway say that the most they
can earn at it is from 3s. to 4s. per day. As
provisions are locally high-priced, the men have
resolved to knock off work altogether. Fancy
navvying at 18s. per week !

Dear Bulletin, —Your sub - leader f
March sth, on “Trade Marks and Treason”'
founded upon a false assumption. The Melbon 18
lager beer company never “appropriated” 116
German label, but merely invented one to fool fc] a
palates of a few local consumers. So with tl' 6
offending hat-factory, which didn’t forge a l6
special brand, but simply beguiled the
stove-piper into supposing that he bore a
burden. In neither case was a trade-mark stole
or an established title usurped. This is the noil
that strikes one who considers the recent Custom
prosecutions in Melbourne—the new English
Trades Mark Act is intended to protect Britis/
industries in some small measure, whilst the new
Victorian Act is worked for the sole benefit of
importers.—F.
Pall Mall Gazette says that Caringtou
would make “an ideal chairman of Londo
County Council.” The Pall Mall doesn’t know
this shallow-minded but much-advertised person
who, however smart he may be at seeing how
the cat jumps, only possesses real intellect
enough to incite him to come in out of the wet
Carington, by “faking” things in advance and
being “ghosted,” was cunning enough to conceal
from the general public of Australia, the diminu-
tiveness of the poor little brain which found a
roosting-place between the top of his yawn and
the bottom of his hair-parting. But there were
two or three occasions on which he clean “ g ave
himself away ”in N.S.W. A deputation once ap-
proached him on a momentous question, and
aware that they were coming, he got somebody—
probably the Lieut.-Governor —to fix up an
answer, reviewing, in a way, the arguments which
he understood the deputation intended using.
However, the deputation didn’t use the arguments
which Carington’s shadow had replied to, and so
Lord Yavvnington found himself “ in tiie soup"”
However, not daring to trust himself to say any-
thing extempore, he read off to the astonished
deputation the bulky MS. with which he had been
provided, and which had as much relevance to
the points at issue as the Paroo mosquitoes
have to a Scotch scratehing-post, and when
the astounded deputation began to interjecr
“ But, your Excellency !” he told them in
effect to “shut up” and said that they were
rude. Carington has all the tricks of the race-
course smarty, or the pet monkey at the Zoo,
but the person who would class such a transparent
and incompetent humbug in the same year or
hemisphere as the Earl of Rosebery would couple
Oscar Wilde and Goethe, or the poet Close and
William Shakespeare.

HOW AN AMERICAN DISCOVERY HAS
AWAKENED EUROPE.
Tub credit is dub to a Citizen of tub United States
BUT TUB BENEFITS ARE SHARED BY THE ENTIRE WORLD.
Considerable excitement exists among phy-
sicians in Europe as to the nature of tile most
dangerous of all modern diseases, and whether it
can be cured.
Ten years ago, Bright’s Disease of the kidneys
was pronounced incurable by almost the entire
medical faculty. To-day, many of them are in
doubt, and nearly all the most advanced declare,
that it not only can be regulated, but absolutely
cured.
High authorities have taken steps to ascertain
the exact views of the leading physicians of
Europe on the subject, and some of their opinions,
condensed, are given herewith :
Sir Morrell Mackenzie, one of the leading
physicians of England, said : —“ I would say,
generally, that Bright’s Disease is curable, but the
man or woman who is troubled with it must be
careful of every action, almost of every thought.”
Dr. William Edward Robson, Physician of the
Royal Navy, declared: —“Like the majority of
ray professional brethren, I have long regarded
Bright’s Disease as incurable. I think so no longer.
Hundreds of my patients have been cured by the
use of Warner’s “ Safe” Cure, and I am willing to
acknowledge and recommend thus frankly the
value of this great remedy. ’
Dr. Andrew Wilson, of the Royal Society of
Edinburgh, asserts: —“Warner’s “Safe” Cure is
of a safe character and perfectly reliable.”
Dr. Hoesch of Berlin, says :—“ I have used
Warner’s “Safe ” Cure myself as I have been the
victim, on taking the least cold, of rheumatism
and palpitation of the heart. This sensibility has
disappeared, also the action of the heart is
quieter.”
Mr. Gustav Weber, of Dessau, Germany,
affirms : —“ By the use of fifteen bottles of
Warner’s “Safe” Cure, my general health has
been restored, and 1 have completely cured myself
of Bright’s Disease of the kidneys.”
Dr. E. H. Whirsel-Huerlimann, of Whipeengen,
Switzerland, makes the following remarkable
assertion :—“ lam a living example that Bright’s
Disease is curable. I suffered with that complaint
for years, and know that improvement in appetite
and strength have been caused through the use of
Warner's “ Safe ” Cure.”
Dr. Dugardid Beaumetz, of the Academy of
Medicine, Paris, affirmed “That the danger in
Bright’s Disease results from the crippled con-
dition of the kidneys, which no longer take out of
the system poisons resulting from organic sub-
stances. The treatment consists in helping the
insufficient action of the kidneys.”
The above facts prove what has been persistently
asserted by 11. H. Warner and Co. for years, that
Bright’s Disease not only can, but has been cured,
and that permanently, and in countless instances.
The moral of all this is that no time should be lost
in applying this guaranteed remedy in any case of
kidney or urinary trouble.

(FOR THE BULLETIN.)

n d a message—“ Come out to the back of the
out at the stake-yards by rising of sun ;

unnt and saddle it was to the rolling of curses,
Snatching whip, snatching spurs, where they hung
& on the nail; , A ,
In his wrath old MTvor, head stockman, turned
Spitting'oaths with his head ’neath the flap of his
last hole in the girth with his teeth
I’lien "a hand on the pommel, a quick catch of
breath, , , . ,
. lift of the body, a swing to the right,
And ten half-broken nags with ten riders astraddle
\Ve sped, arrow-swift, for the heart of the night.
Thud of hoofs, thud of hearts, breath of man,
breath of beast,
With MTvor in front, but the rest heel to flank,
C„ , ve rode in a bunch down the steep river bank,
Churning up the black tide in the shallows like
Througif'the coolabahs, out on the plain, it
increased ,
Till we swung with the stride of the dingo-paek,
swooping
On the scent of weak mother with puny calf
drooping. . ... .
Staring eyes, swaying forms o er the saddle-bow
stooping, , . ,
With the wind in our shirts, grip of knee, grip of
LosingHground, falling back, creeping forward
again.
Behind us the low line of dark coolabah,
Overhead a sky spangled by planet and star ;
And to left, on our shoulder, the mighty Cross
flaring,
While afoot the quick rhythm of hoof-beats
disturbs . . ,
The moist silence of grasses and salty-leaved
herbs.
Steering on by the stars, over hollow and crest,
Tingling eyes—looking ou’t through a film of tears
From the slap of the wind—over forward-pricked
ears,
Over forehead and nose stretching out for the
west —
And into the face of the sombre night staring.
Threading in, threading out, through a maze of
sand rises
That spring either side, loom a moment, then flee—
Dim hillocks of herbage and sun-blasted tree—
Till again a dark streak of far timber arises,
And anon, through the thick of a lignum swamp
tearing
Bare tendrils, back-springing, switch sharp on the
knee.
Plain again, and again, with the speed of the
wind,
The long miles in the front join their comrades
behind ;
Then a sound in our ears, like to far summer
thunder,
Or the booming of surf in a southerly gale ;
And we shouted aloud, each to each in our
wonder,
For we knew that those beasts must have come
fast and far,
That they moaned, as the breaking of waves on a
bar.
But behold, overhead, the dark sky had grown
pale
White, clouded by blue, as is newly-skimrncd
milk,
And the dawn-spiders floated on threads of floss-
silk
As the guards of the sun drew aside the thick veil
And made ready to fling the dawn-portals asunder.
Still that sound swelled and rolled, thrilling deep
on the air,
Calling long, calling loud in the ear of each steed,
Bringing courage and strength in the hour of need,
And light’ning the weight of the burdens they
bare.
But that moment, behind us, upshot a red glare
As the sun swept the sky with a roseate sponge,
And MTvor’s blue-roan gave a rear, and a plunge,
A half sob, and so fell, like an overripe pear.
Not a rein did we pull, not a stride did we stay,
Speeding onward, and speeding, for long we could
hear,
Turning often aside, through the dew-laden
grasses
To crop a sweet mouthful; we needed no glasses
To see it was Fogarty, once and again,
And again did we hail, yet lie never looked round,
Neither made the least motion of hearing the
sound,
Riding on as a man who should ride in his sleep,
Or as one in the web of some deep-woven charm;
Bo he came through the grass—his horse striding
breast-deep
With a woman held close in the crook of his arm.
And her hair, all unbound, rippled over his
shoulder,
Bead black; and her brow—where the sweat of
fierce pain
Had dried—was brown-tinged as bronze is, but
colder,
Ah, many times colder, and as ho pulled rein,
He unwrapped saddle-blanket in which he had
rolled her,
And 10, the gay sunlight lit ominous stain,
Old Mac.’s maledictions ring loud in our rear,
As wo rode in hot haste from the incoming day.
Then all sudden, and strangely, we came face to
face
With the lead of the cattle, and 10, our long race
Was run out, and we drew up the horses, all
panting
In stress of the chase, and yet ready for more ;
And our eager ears drank in that thunderous roar,
While we watched the red squadrons come over
the levels
As if view-hulloed by a pack of night-devils ;
Cow and calf chasing heifer and lumbering steer,
With their grey, dripping nostrils, and eyes wide
with fear,
As if Burgess’ Cob* followed hard on their rear.
So we blocked them, and 10, the new sun laid a
slanting
Red finger on one who rode over the plain,
Steed treading full slowly, head drooping, slack
rein,
Where a murderous bullet had torn a blue vein,
And let out her young life, in a warm crimson
rain.
Then gently, he laid his sad load on the ground,
And with sorrowing glances we gathered around.
Then he turned to the west—with his eyes all
aflame,
With his brawny fists raised, calling witness from
Heaven ;
On his shoulder and flank the dark blood of the
slain—
And he hurled his curse back on the place whence
he came :
A loud curse, and a threat, that he yet would
stand even
With those of Monkyra who wrought this foul
shame.

AN OPTICAL EFFECT.
BULLETIN MAN (to intoxicated visitor from
country ) : “ Yon haven’t seen last
week’s issue? Why don’t you read
THE BULLETIN regularly?"
INTOXICATED VISITOR: “ Y’shee, I always
read it when I’m at home in the bush ;
but when I come to town I — hi c—don’t
shoe anythingsh.”
Though, to tell the God’s truth, we’d have done
just the same
In their place, and have reckoned it nothing but
right;
For the black girl and Fogarty quietly crept
On the Monkyra men in the dead of the night,
And it happ’ed that the watchman was weary,
and slept.
So the gin, who no doubt was a game little pullet,
Slipped in, arid brought both their night horses
away;
While Fogarty started the cattle that lay
On the camp, and the trick was bold it suc-
ceeded ;
For the Monkyra men, when their cattle stam-
peded,
Had nothing to send in pursuit but a bullet.
Yet that was as much as the little gin needed,
She made no great fuss though, nor murmured,
nor cried,
Only rode on the right of her lord till she died.
Her life ended well, nothing scamped, or by
halves;
Where she went, who can tell? But ice branded
the calves.

Personal Items.
Milner Stephen, the faith-healer, is back
in Melbourne.
Sir Frederick Leighton’s father was a Jew,
and lately died, aged 93.
Speaker Davies (Vic ) retires from poiitu s
to face his private affairs.
Dean Macartney, of Melbourne, will be
94 years of age on April 12.
To which of his cousins is Prince George
to be married ? Alice’s daughter, or Helena’s ?
More rickets, anyhow.
Professor Pearson gets five guineas for
every leader he writes in Melbourne Age. No
other man has ever got such a fee in Australia.
Chief Justice Darlcy (N.S.W.) and Sir
Bryan O’Loghlen (Vic.) went the Munster cir-
cuit together. Their bags used to be choke-full of
briefs.
G. N. Taylor, the Laud Credit Bank
swindler, Melbourne, wept copiously in the dock.
Ho wept Judge Hodges down to a two-years’
sentence.
Rev. W. Gardner, of Bombala, N.S.W.,
claims to have traced Lord Jersey’s pedigree back
to Julius Caesar. His Ex. has certainly crossed
the Rubicund.
There are in Sydney two C.M.G.’s who
make it a point to miss the funeral of no-one who
is anybody. On this principle, how many will
attend their funerals ?
“George Augustus Sala,” says a recent
paragraph, “is always known by his white waist-
coat.” When in bathing-costume he is always
known by his red nose.
Fraud Larkin’s last public appearance, as
the boss of South Melbourne, was, in the chair, at
the local football-club banquet. Says he : “ Boys !
Go straight, and have nothing to do with the
bookmakers.”
J. L. Sullivan offers to fight any white
man. The Bulletin accepts the wager, on
behalf of James Hennessey, of Cognac. James
is dead, but he is quite good enough to fight John
L. Sullivan.
Williain-Colbeck, at onetime sub-editor of
Sydney Daily Telegraph, and latterly editor of
the Cape Register, S.A., is dead, aged 40 years.
Colbcck was a man of brains. Brains too often
mean nerves.
Ex-Canon Whitington who is going to
Macgregorland as a missionary, was once law-
reporter for the S.A. Register, Adelaide. He
took to the church ; a successor gravitated to gaol
in Melbourne. Both doing well, thanks.
Parkes has lectured before the bankers of
Sydney, on “the position of N.S. Wales in the
future Australian Federation.” “Taking one
consideration with another,” banker Abraham
Friedman should have occupied the chair on the
occasion.

Mrs. Gordon-Baillie is already out of
prison. As she got five years she must have
obtained a considerable remission. Wherever she
goes she will be able to whistle the most godly man
down from the topmost bough of the loftiest tree.
There never lived a much finer woman.

The meaning whereof will be a mystery to anyone

Parkes, the other night, related that when
Wright (now plain M.P.) was “Works, ’in the
N.S. W. Soudan-donkey Ministry, he was re*
minded by a deputation, which was mounting the
stairs leading to his office, that it had an appoint-
ment with him. Wright worriedly replied—
“ Tut, tut, there is no time for anything of that
kind now; 1 am engaged in saving the dear old
Mother Country ! ”
Editor Curnow, of the S.M. Herald, at a
recent banquet, gave Lis definition of an editor.
“ To his mind, the editor of a newspaper was a
sort of speaking-tube or telephone, that gathered
together and carried on the messages of the great
public.” In other words, the public is the pig,
and the editor is the sausage-machine, and the
S.M. Herald is the finished sausage that comes
out at the other cud.
Mrs. Morriset, wife of the wry-mouthed
Commandant at Norfolklsland in the thirties, who
was also colonel of the 48th Regt.. died last week
at North Sydney, aged 90 years. One of her sons
was a superintendent of police, and another a
police-magistrate. We believe she outlived all
her family. The Colonel got the nick name from
having been shot, in the Peninsula, through the
mouth. His name does not carry a blessing with
it amongst the old hands.
E. H. Herrington, who not so very long
ago relinquished the Port Adelaide News. S.A.,
has been in his day—telegraph-operator, store-
keeper, local preacher, shorthand writer, temper-
ance lecturer, mesmerist, divining-rod man,
artist, journalist, M.P., and Minister of the
Crown. He still retains the power of blushing
clean through his peach-blossom countenance,
away down the lankiness of his profile right to his
Adam’s-apple, and clean round to his bumps of
destructiveness.
The late millionaire John Harris, of
Shane’s Park, N.S W., was once, in his droving
days, standing, roughly dressed, outside a mining
township pub., when a flash digger drove up in a
irap, and threw him the reins to hold. Jock took
them without saying a word, and waited. When
the digger climbed back into his bugery, lie threw
•lock a “fiver.” Jock flung the note back, with
the remark that five pounds wasu t enough in
those flush times for holding a fellow’s horse for
five minutes. The digger was so flabbergasted at
Jock’s front that ho “ slung him a tenner,” which
Jock calmly pocketed.
What People Say.
T. IRVINE, ESQ., TATHAM, RICHMOND
RIVER, N.S.W.
Writes under date February 2ft, 1801;—“I
write to let you know that I am a patron of your
wonderful Clements Tonic ; I have been a great
sufferer, and have read of Mrs. Moeller’s cure in
the newspapers; her case was nothing to mine,
but, thank God, through her letter I am cured,
and after the doctors had pronounced sentence of
death on me. lam very grateful to Mrs. Midler
for her letter. I have great pleasure in adding
my testimony to that of the many others cured
of serious diseases by the use of Clements Tonic ;
I have suffered terribly from liver and kidney
disease. Two years ago my strength began
rapidly to decline, I had dull headache, completely
lost my appetite, and was almost blind (failure of
sight is a symptom of Bright’s disease.) I
had tiger claw cramps in the calves of my legs,
and severe rheumatic pains, followed by diarrhoea;
strength continued to fail, accompanied by
extreme pallor of the face, puffed under the eyes,
and persistent swelling of legs, knees and ankles,
and my whole body was swollen to a wonderful
size ; sharp shooting pains pierced the heart, and
frequently chills and fever attacked me. The
swelling was so bad that I was afraid to even
drink a glass of water. I consulted a doctor, and
he examined my water, and he said it was a bad
case of liver disease, and also Bright’s disease of
the kidneys ; he prescribed medicine and liniments
with mustard baths. I said, “ Doctor, if you will
cure me I will pay you what you like.” He
replied ; “ I can hold out no hope for you.” I
said, “ That is very hard.” “ I never like to take
a man’s money,” said he, “without telling him
the truth, and I will come to you at any hour of
the night you may send for me.” He thought I
should soon die. I gave his treatment a fair trial,
but the swelling of my body increased so much
that I had to remain in bed. The pains increased
and extended all down the side, and my eyesight
was now almost completely gone. I consulted
another eminent medical man, who after sounding
me and testing my urine, said I had Bright’s
disease. I asked if he could cure me. He said
he would do his best, and if I would continue his
medicine I should improve. I did so, and it had
ut little more effect than water. Two friends of
mine called my attention to Mrs. Moeller’s letter
in the paper, who had been cured of the same
disease by Clements Tonic. I procured a supply,
and taking it strictly in accordance with the
directions, the swelling began to go down. I
kept up the use of Clements Tonic, and now,
thank God, I am cured ; my swellings have all
subsided ; my eyesight is as good as ever. I took
a good many bottles, but that is nothing, as IT
has cured me, and I cannot describe its value.”
Mr. Irvine’s case needs no comment. We have
no need to eulogise our remedy when disinterested
people give such endorsement as the above.

Society
The Windsor (Vic.) brick-and -cement
murderer was said to have kept a restaurant
in Sydney. It would be interesting to figure
out the number of murderers who have, during
some portion of their lives, been identified
with restaurants and sausage-shops. Years ago,
the present writer and an aged companion went
into a Sydney restaurant, behind the counter of
which was a cross-eyed woman selling mutton-
pies. “By Jove!” exclaimed the old fellow,
between ids mouthfuls, “ where have I seen that
face before?” And then, after half-an-hour’s
memorising, “ I have it. That’s Sarah Gale, the
paramour and criminal confederate of Greenacre,
whom I saw tried with him in London.” And
so she was. She had married respectably (she’s
now in heaven, sweet soul), and had taken to the
minced-meat business.
# # #
De Quincoy’s imaginary lecturer on
“ Murder as one of the Fine Arts ” was named
by the author, “Williams.” In Wilkie Collins’s
“The Haunted Hotel,” a murdered body was
hidden beneath a hearthstone. If the Windsor
(Vie.) murderer, Williams, had only been able to
leave a walking ghost about the scen£ of his
butchery, the coincidences would have been still,
more faithful to literary legends.
* * *
A servant girl at the Federal Coffee Palace,
Melbourne, stated that a couple answering to the
description of “Williams” and his murdered
companion stayed there for a few days and “had
the appearance of being married people.” That is
to say, the man was “studiously unkind and
insulting to the lady.”
* * *
When a big mail steamer is coming out
from England with a crowd of passengers, there
is always on board the inevitable girl on her way
to marry the old sweetheart whom she hasn’t seen
for several years—the sweetheart who has been
working to make a home for her, and who has at
last written telling her to come out and be bis
pansy-blossom for evermore. First, that girl is
sea sick. Secondly, she is physically well, but is
reticent and romantic, and sits round on deck at
night looking at the moon, and extracts letters
from hidden recesses in the vicinity of her bosom,
and looks surreptitiously at a photograph.
Thirdly, she feels the want of a confidant,
and tells every soul on board, barring only
the stokers and the deck-sweep, all about her little
love-story. There was a girl of that sort on board
a recent mail-boat, and her tale, as she related it
to the captain and all the officers and the under-
stewardess, was a very touching one. “It is six
years since I saw Harry,” she would say sadly,
“ and I really don’t know if I love him now. But
if when we meet I feel a thrill here” and she
would lay her hand regretfully somewhere on the
region of her waist, “ I’ll know that it’s all right.”
When Harry came down to meet her there were
82 people standing about to know if she felt a
thrill, but she was bundled into a cab and driven
away so hurriedly that the point was left un-
settled.

—Exchange.
over .£20,000 is 5 per cent, to children and grand-
children, 10 per cent, to other blood relations, and
13 per cent, to strangers in blood. If a man deeds
his property as a gift while alive, he lias to pay
the probate-duty all the same, for the deed cannot
be registered till this is paid. Such has been the
law here since 1885, when one of the queer misers
in Auckland died. His name fitly expressed the
hardness of his nature, and, although he used to
look up even the dry bread when lie thought his
children had eaten enough, he could not bear to
seo them deprived of the probate-dutv. After a
long illness, borne with Christian fortitude, and
by no moans extravagant use of medicine, he
signed the deeds of gift to his different children,
on which deeds a stamp-duty of ten-shillings only
was payable, and a few hours later expired. The
probate-duty on nearly a quarter-of-a million was
thereby saved, but the tax-collectors arched their
backs and sharpened their claws to prevent such
a thing happening again.—Yours, Conveyancer,
Tauranga, Maoriland.
# # *
A swell of the first water lately called
upon a Southern inspector of prisons, and intro-
duced himself as the Governor of a South African
Gaol, was well received, and invited the inspector
to his “club.” Social courtesies were daily
reciprocated, now at the inspector’s office, anon at
the club. The inspector sounded his friend’s
praises, and introduced him everywhere as a good
fellow on tour. But, one day, when the “good
fellow ” was at the inspector’s office, a clerk from
the principal gaol jostled against him on the
stairs—and recognised him as a discharged convict
from one of the inspector’s own gaols.
* * *
“ Poor thing ! ” exclaimed Aspinall, Q.C.,
when old Jack Williams, the now just-deceased
detective, remarked, in a burglary case, “ I seen
an ole maid (hole made) in the ceiling.” Aspinall
would make his sycophants roar even by saying:
“ And, gentlemen, one witness is this venerable
Williams, who, I observe, has donned a brand-
new red necktie for the auspicious occasion.”
* # •
Neddy Lovel, N.S.W.’s oldest constable,
has died after 40 years’ service. He had never
had a wife—and had only one “ stripe” when lie
died. This may be read allegorically.
* * #
A certain squatter, lately in Parlia-
ment, took up land per dummy—said dummy
was an Arab, named Selim. By-and-bye the
squatter wished for an additional conditional
purchase, but said Selim being legally “ full up”
it couldn’t be done. To overcome this difficulty
the squatter brings Selim to the nearest town,
has him baptised, and thus makes a Christian
(name James Brown) of him; then goes back to
the local land-office and takes up an additional
C.P. in James Brown’s name. Passed by Land
Eoard.
Nearly every restaurant-keeper in Sydney
will remember a certain prosperous-looking, fat
man who used to dine, in a promiscuous way, all
over the city. He was well-dressed, and wore a
watch-chain, a shiny bat, and an umbrella. The
umbrella was old, and moth-eaten in a thousand
holes, and its ribs were all stove-in and splintered
—it was a sort of ash-heap umbrella, in fact, but the
handle was all right, and it was nicely done up in
an oilskin case. The owner lived on this umbrella.
He would trot into a restaurant and eat as if
there was no hereafter, and then ho would walk
up to the pay-counter and feel in his pockets for
the half-crown. Just here a look of pained sur-
prise would come over his countenance, and he
would remark that, by a most astonishing and
unforeseen combination of circumstances, he had
somehow come out without any money. “If you
will allow me,” he would remark cheerfully, “I
will leave my umbrella as security until to-
morrow.” Then the proprietor would take stock
of him, and he looked so solvent that he invari-
ably declined the umbrella, and remarked that
the customer could pay any time lie was passing.
Next day he would go and tender the umbrella in
another restaurant, with the same result. He at«
his way in this manner right along Pitt street,
Sydney, from one end to the other. Next, ha
used-up the side streets, and ate them all bare,
from Bridge-street to the railway-station ; he also
dined successfully through Castlereagh and Eliza-
beth-streets. When they were finished he started
on George-street, and fed systematically from
Circular Quay as far as Bathurst-street, and then
the scheme fell through. At the place where ho
had his last dinner a hard-featured lady, when he
had explained the circumstances, took the
umbrella from him without the slightest compunc-
tion, and he was so broken-up by the loss of his
stock-in-trade that he never, so far as we know,
made a fresh start. The gamp that goes too
often to the dining-room is sure to be seized at
last.
# # #
A German who suicided a few weeks ago
left a letter behind him, stating that he did it
because life was “too monotonous.” Which,
after all, is about the most logical reason that
could well be devised. We often feel that we
will suicide some day on account of the building
across the street, simply because we have seen
the miserable old thing so often. Yet we suppose
no jury will have pluck enough to bring in a
verdict that we “ Died of the house opposite.”
# # #
Up South one P.M. is up before Court on
a fraud summons, issued at the suit of a grocer.
Another P.M. looms ahead as a divorce-suit co-
respondent ; a third is involved in some land-
transactions with a fish-like smell. A fourth is a
hopeless sot.

The coolest man up to date is he who ex-
plained in Sydney police-court last week, that he
could not pay his wife’s alimony because he was
keeping another woman.
# * *
The case of the girl who, the other day,
appeared in a Sydney police-dock arrayed in the
uniform of a bluejacket from the Orlando who had
given it her “ to be photographed in,” recalls an
old-time Melbourne occurrence when the late
Henry Gillbee, a major of Volunteers, got into
trouble over the fact that a tipsy female friend of
his annexed his martial sword and plumes and
proudly marched down street with them.
Melbourne tradesman: “I’m going to

* # *
A Melbourne newspaper, answering a
correspondent, the other day, said that a man
could not marry his wife’s daughter, but one of
the most prominent Melbourne men did, and has
a largo grown-up family. Of course the reasons
for and against are the same as in regard to
marriage with a wife’s sister —to do which, Rev.
Punshon went from England to Canada.

Age and the Evening Standard (Melb.) were to
avoid identification with the vagaries of their
respective peculative artists Noel de Leon and
Henry Scott, each keeping its own name out of
the case of its late employe, and publishing that of
the other journal in the usual capital letters when
the “other man ” was concerned.

A PATRIOTIC DITTY.
I deal in stocks and shares, and stand
By all our institutions great,
And ho who owns a block of land
I deem a saviour of the State ;
But the working man’s a shady lot—
Says the Young Victorian Patriot.
I do support the Dual Vote,
For brain is brain, and earth is earth,
A tall silk hat and gloves denote
The gent of special weight and worth,
Which the working man, of course, is not—
Says the Young Victorian Patriot.
Though labour, in its proper place,
I dc not wish to underrate,
’Tis clear to me in any case
That Capital maintains the State.
A straw for democratic “ rot”—
Says the Young Victorian Patriot.
I hold that in the shipping strike
A traitor spirit was displayed ;
The rates of pay employers like
Are naturally best for trade.
The working man’s-(no matter what)—
Says the Young Victorian Patriot.
God save the Queen and bless the Squire,
And all our institutions great;
The kind of person I admire
Alone is useful to the State,
So all the rest can go to pot—
Says the Young Victorian Patriot.
LYRICS OF THE STAGE.
(The Mirth-Provoking “Dam!”)
The drama holds a mirror bright
To Human Nature’s gaze,
Inciting us to love the right
And turn from wicked ways ;
The author strives with caustic pen
To prick a social sham,
But always seems most witty when
His funny-man says “ Dam ! ”
One source of pleasure never dries,
But emulates the Brook,
All moth and rust that word defies—
’Tis ever in the “ book.”
It follows plays around, ye ken,
Like Mary’s little lamb,
And worlds unborn will cackle when
The funny-man says “ Dam ! ”
The action may be hanging fire,
The story going slow ;
The villain’s victims don’t expire
And finish up their woe.
Perchance the hero now and then
Is overdosed with “ jam ”
But O ! the house looks happy when
The funny-man says “Dam ! ”
It pleases old and young alike ;
The sinner or the saint,
That exclamation seems to strike
As wonderfully quaint.
You see mannna with daughters ten
And pa (who’s standing Sam)
Exchanging mirthful glances when
The funny-man says “ Dam ! ”
One day I hope to score a hit;
I have a play unique—
’Twill suit the stalls and charm the pit,
And make the circle shriek.
I read it in my lonely den,
And, here and there, I am
Inserting fresh occasions when
The funny-man says “ Dam ! ”
Melbourne.
E.F.

felt in all his pockets, and there wasn’t a cent in
any of them. Then the publican remarked that it
didn’t matter—he could pay. him next evening—
and the rich man drank his beer and went away.
That was five months ago, and he has never been
in since. When he leaves his office in the evening
he walks down Bridge-street to George-street,
goes along George-street, walks up Hunter-street,
and catches his omnibus in Pitt-street. It is only
twenty or thirty yards to the ’bus in a straight
line, and it is a-quarter of a mile the way he goes
now, but if he took the straight line the publican
might possibly rush out as he was passing and ask
him for the threepence. Up to date he has walked
neatly thirty miles to save those three coppers,
and has wrecked about two pair of boots, which
he will probably make good by reducing his
clerk’s salary. Thus does the great virtue of
Thrift once more come uppermost.

The moral of a recent Sydney case is this :
Never insultingly address a strange girl in the
street, because, after she has banged you in the
eye with her gamp, you may find yourself at her
feet, begging her to accept a £250-allotment to
square the case.

The Victorian Wesleyan Conference re-
ports that during the past year 14 Chinese have
been admitted into the visible church. The next
thing necessary will be to disinfect the church.

What a farce to administer the oath to an
evidently tutored child ! The other day, in Mel-
bourne, an intelligent little lad, asked where ho
would go if he told a lie, answered “ to the Refor-
matory.”
At Birmingham, (Eng.), a well-known
solicitor, his wife, the solicitor’s dog and the wife’s
pet donkey are all buried in the one grave, and the
balance of their combined fortunes —after paying
for the monument —goes to a Dogs’ home. The
lawyer and the dog and the donkey were lovely
in their lives and in death they are not divided.

North Melbourne is getting more famous
every day as the paradise of the “ maintenance ”
case. The other day a woman, who brought her
husband before the Bench, confessed that her
lawyers had advised her to live in North Mel-
bourne for a time before bringing on the case.
# # #
Dog-meat is the latest American remedy
for consumption, and is said to be a great success.
A young lady at San Francisco, who was suffering
from phthisis, ate seven dogs in two weeks, and
the results were most gratifying. The Bulletin
strongly recommends this treatment for two
reasons—first, it may possibly cure the consump-
tive, and, second, it will stop the dog from barking
at night. We know 120 dogs which urgently want
to be eaten in a good cause, and we also know
four consumptives who owe their disease to a
cold, caught while trying to shift a mongrel out
of the back premises on a damp night. There is a
great moral in all this. In future, when a man
can’t sleep owing to an excited stray pup in the
yard, he will simply rush out with a kni r e
and fork and eat the animal. Then the
dog himself will be a guarantee against any
bad effects which may arise through flying
round in an abbreviated garment amid the dewy
grass, and the only danger seems to be that the
animal might turn out to be a St. Bernard, and
consequently be too large to consume, and an
unfinished dog might not be a sufficient protection,
# # *
A circular issued by a Melbourne Customs
official invited certain pressmen to join in a certain
trip down the Bay. It was carefully mentioned
in the last line that “light refreshments would be
provided, but no set meal!” The old tradition :
a square feed is the price of the Press.
* * #
An historic relic, a copy of the General
Orders, printed, published and bound by convicts
in 1802 (N.S.W.) was sold at Sir John Hay’s sale
for £25. The question naturally arises how this
valuable work, unquestionably at one time the
property of the Government, got into private
hands? The only other known copy is in the
British Museum.

A Melbourne “ common ” jury failed to
convict confessed-fraud Larkin and his secretary,
Cleary. The Crown will now empanel a “special”
jury. The difference between a “ common ” and a
“special” juror is the difference between an
annual rental rated at £2O and one rated at £OO.
The man of “ thrift ” again ! He is supposed to
have more intelligence than his poorer neighbour.
In many cases he has less. In many instances, lie
has a redundance of commercial cunning and a
secret sympathy with successful commercial fraud,
so long as neither he, nor his immediate friends,
are among the defrauded. A “special” jury is
sometimes a “ packed ” jury.
# * #
The will of the late John McCulloch, a S.A.
squatter, was thus worded :
I will and bequeath all my property that I own at pre-
sent, and everything that I may obtain hereafter, to my
wife and her children.
In a “friendly interpretation suit,” the Court dfc*
cided that the term “ her children” only applied
to the issue of the McCulloch marriage, and did
not include any future issue by any subsequent
marriage which the widow might contract. A life-
estate in the widow, with remainder to her
McCulloch offspring at her decease, was decreed.
* . * «
William of Germany distinguished himself
by not taking the trouble to be deeply afflicted
when his cousin Clarence died. The sad news
reached his Majesty whilst he was out shooting
rabbits, but instead of tottering home in a
paroxysm of grief, with the crown on the back of
his head and a damp imperial handkerchief
waving in the wind, he went on shooting rabbits
as before. Also, the heartless monarch neglected
to attend any divine se’rviee for the benefit of his
relative’s soul, and finally, he reduced the usual
period of Court anguish from three weeks to ten
days. All of which conduct has deeply wounded
the feelings of thousands of superior middle-class
ladies in. England, and if the Emperor William
were to die to-morrow they would not go into
mournirig for him The least bit. At any rate, they
would not buy neiv mourning for him —so, there
now !

A Bulletin correspondent thus gossips :
The novelist’s conception of a detected murderer is not
home out in real life, according to the writer’s experi-
onoc. I remember when going up coast in the Egmont
somewhere about 77 we had Cunningham, the murderer
of “Frank the Austrian,” aboard in charge of a young
Brisbane policeman, who gave him a good deal of freedom
during daylight, calling him below quietly at dusk and
putting the “ darbies ” on. The constable camped in the
bunk above Cunningham, and, in answer to my inquiries,
informed me that he always slept as peacefully as a child,
He was a quiet-looking young fellow, and most of us
for’ard were assured of hisinnocence,yet,crebeing“turned
off,” he confessed to the cowardly murder of poor Frank,
Watsort, the Cowl-Cowl murderer was employed at
Carrathool, erecting a slaughter-yard for John Parr, about
12 months before the deed was committed. He camped
in the hotel stable-loft, and after he left, the hotel groom
found a most artistically-made gag under the bags which
Watson had had for a hipper. It consisted of a sheep-
shank bone, neatly notched for the whipcord strings, and
was passed round among “all-hands” ere anyone
“ tumbled ”to its use. I was on the Gunbar road when
they were bringing Watson down on the coach. Although
the Cowl-Cowl murder had been the talk of the district
Charley* 0 Watson, 1 the cShST fencer. °Xh thf
Watson suspected of the crime. My employer had a
parcel on the coach, and as I went up to receive it, I
caught sight of Watson inside. “Ilulloa, Charley!”!
exclaimed, “where are you bound for?” —when, half-
extending my hand to “shake,” I sighted the iron orna-
ments he wore on wrists and ankles. You may be sure I
took in the situation quickly enough then, though with
such an eerie feeling that “ you might have knocked me
down with a feather.” Anyhow, that question of mine
was rather a poser for Master Watson. —“ Tub Palmer.”

W. A. Murray, land-grabber and ex-
M.H.R., got lost recently on his property at
Waikato (M.L.), and wandered about for several
days feeding miserably on roots, at the imminent
risk of his life. Nobody should be allowed to own
enough property to get lost upon it. When a
man grabs a great solitude, and then is bushed in
the middle thereof and dies because he has seized
so much land that he can’t find his way out of it—
well, he must have some cheerful ideas in his last
moments, one would think.

sittings after the N.S.W. Divorce-Extension Bill
gets through.

George Attenborough, prince of British
,s,* , c ,
pawnbrokers he . was the patron of several
Anglican church livings, which had fallen into
his hands cheap—is dead. He was a good friend
to the nobility and gentry, always ready to take
th e family jewels in pledge, and buy superfluous
wedding-presents at an alarming sacrifice In
some cases, the same wedding-present would pass
through Attenborough s hands halr-a-dozen times
before settling down to private life and domestic
use. As soon as a bridegroom sold the claret jug,
or whatnot, a customer would come along and buy
it for presentation to another happy pair, who, in
due course, would do the usual deal with Atten-
borough, and so on.
That medical witness who climbs cheer-
fully into the box, and testifies that the speck of
gore on the prisoner’s boot is human blood, ami
gets him hanged accordingly, had better take a
back seat for evermore. An eminent British
medical authority announces that no scientist
under the sun can tell human blood from the
blood of any other mammal. The difference con-
sistsentirely in the size of the corpuscles; in the case
of man they measure generally about one-four-
thousandth part of an inch in diameterand one-ten-
thousandth of an inch thick; in the case of clogs,
horses, and the like they are about the same
thickness, and perhaps an eighth of that four-
thousandth part of an inch wider round the waist.
When a crime has been committed the bush doctor
—the man who removes legs with an axe and
treats every complaint with blue pills—gets an
old, dry spot, and having measured the invisible
corpuscles with a two-foot rule, he swears that,
“ to the best of his belief,” it is a splash of human
blood. The court credits his statement because
he is a doctor, and he is telling the strict truth
because he does believe it to be human blood, and
he believes that way because the policeman who
brought the spot round to his shop believed it also,
and yet he is just as good an authority on the
subject as a bricklayer or a sign-painter, and an
ordinary butcher is probably more reliable than
any of them. The blood-spot business, like the
Tipperary-boot-heel-tracking business, is played-
out.
Dear Bulletin, —So W. J. Sowden, the
late head of the S.A. Register reporting staff,
goes to London as cable-liar for the S.A. Tiber,
Melbourne Age, and Sydney D.T. Sowden is a
native of Victoria, and was once a compositor and
always a teetotaller ! His predecessor, “ Corney ”
Proud, made a sinful pile with Broken Hill
shares, and is now an Adelaide broker. He was
the victim of the <£3500 robbery of notes from an
Adelaide bank-counter nearly two years ago. As
to other old Register “reps.”—Phil. Benjamin
occupies a commercial position in Sydney ; Jack
Roberts edits the Melbourne Evening Standard,
after a spell with the Argus and another with
N.S.W. Hansard ; Pat Saunders is a leader-
writer on the E. Standard ; D. M. Magill was
recently running a press-agency in Melbourne;
Ered Bathurst is up to'his neck in sharebroking
and runs a Sydney mining journal; Peters is on tl e
Sydney Daily Telegraph ; Skipper still sticks
to Adelaide; old Holden, the octogenarian, yet
holds the editorship of the Riddler page in
the Adelaide Observer, arid only smokes once a
year-on the 4th July; one ex-law reporter —
heaven help him !—is a missionary, while another
has been in gaol and got lost; and an erratic cuss
is still a movable flaneur of eccentric orbit. I
believe the two Days—parson, leader-writer and
paymaster, known reportorially as “Judgment”
Day and “Pay ” Day respectively—still officially
survive. J. C. F. Johnson blossomed consecu-
tively into a comic-paper proprietor, mining specu-
lator, M.P., Minister of Education, and resting
capitalist. Charlick, chess editor, is still champion
of his own roost. I have lost the run of Dusantoy,
formerly of Ballarat, Vic. Let’s moisten the
memories.— The Dipsomaniac.
Rebecca Cohen, a woman who died a
horrible death lately in Sydney hospital, left
behind her a gruesome and pathetic story of the
ways of the “unregistered practitioner ’’who lives
upon the ignorant person that doesn't feel well. Mere
than a year ago—so her own statement is reported
to have run—a cancer started to grow on her
lower lip, and she was urged by the hospital
authorities to have it cut out. She declined, and
went to a certain “Dr.” Hood, in one of the
suburbs, who undertook to cure her without an
operation for the trifling sum of £3 a week.
Finding that his patient was unable to pay this
amount he reduced the charge to £l, and for 12
months made a regular income out of the miser-
able sufferer—in fact, he is said to have col-
lected the money with remorseless regularity till
within a few days of her death. His treatment,
according to the patient’s story, consisted in drop-
ping some strong acid on the cancer —a process
which was just as scientific as if he had treated it
with the actual cautery. Between the cancer and
the medicine alarge hole was gradually burnt from
the mouth right down to the neck, and the flesh at
the sides of this gruesome orifice was all blistered
and destroyed. The doctor, in fact, was getting
on favourably towards removing all the front part
of his client by a process of slow combustion, but
after a year of it the half cooked woman gave him
up and died. The immediate cause of her
death was want of food, her mouth being partly
burnt away and the rest of it so decom-
posed that eating or drinking became practically
impossible. The case is, at time of writing, still
being sorted out in the Coroner’s Court, and the
“ doctor ” complains that the poor mangled woman
didn’t pay him regularly ; also, he states that he
can’t find sundry important documents which, if
he could find them, would utterly demolish all
suspicions, and he “disremembers ” the meaning
of the “A. 8.” which follows his name on his
door-plate. He has some objects in a bottle
which look like oysters, but which really are
cancers extracted by his process from various
grateful patients. Also, he has some valuable
documents in the Maori tongue, and he declares
that he took half-an-ounce of cancerous flesh from
Mrs. Cohen daily, which would make 51b. of
cancer in all. Sundry patients who gave testi-
monials about their “cures” have rolled up to
explain or modify or withdraw their testimonials.
Up to date, the coroner’s jury don’t seem to like
“Dr. Hood.”

Fall Mall Gazette rakes up from th e
American Baptist, Rev. Dr. Hatcher’s account

cunning sarcasm, under the guise of puffery.

Hill mansion, with its glorious Park, noble
garden, and even the kitchen garden and fowleries
roused Hatcher’s gorge, which mounted as he
beheld the superb barouche, pair of unsurpassed
high-steppers, and the liveried coachman “who
lo >ked quite a gentleman,” while the stables were
“ equal in their way to those of Windsor Castle.”
Good old—what shall we say?
Dear Bulletin,— At Laidley, Q., the other
evening, a meeting was convened in the “Victoria
flail” to “support” the Griffilwraith Kanaka
policy. The hall was packed to overflowing, about
MOV) crushing themselves into it somehow. A
riotous Bedlam ensued, in which the Black-labour
advocates were outnumbered at every vote.
Nevertheless, the chairman declared every resolu-
tion unanimously carried. The resolutions:—
1. That tliis meeting views with great satisfaction the
opinions expressed in the manifesto issued by Sir S. W.
Griffith, and pledges itself to support the proposals.
Amendment to same: That this meeting views the return
of Queensland to Black Labour with horror and disgust,
and, also, that they pledge themselves to do their utmost
to prevent such. Motion declared unanimously carried !
2. That, this meeting is of opinion that it would lie to
tiie best interests of the people of Queensland to extend
the term to 15 instead of 10 years, during which it is
proposed that Polynesian labour may be introduced into
Queensland. Declared carried unanimously !!
3. That this meeting is of the opinion that the time has
arrived when all future railways, where practicable, should
he constructed on the land-grant principle. Amendment
to same: That this meeting condemns the Government
from start to finish. Motion declared carried unani-
mously !! !
Cheers for Griffith ; groans for Griffith ; chaos.
An outdoor meeting was afterwards held —“ much
larger and very orderly ” (see Ipswich Advocate
report)—at which the platform was a dray. An
effigy of Griffith was prominently in evidence.
Resolved :
That it is unnecessary and uncalled-for to introduce
black-labour in Queensland.
The Griffith dummy was kerosened, and about to
be burnt, when Constable Fairbrother forbade the
illumination. So the boys made a football of it,
and kicked the effigy to smithereens.—Yours,
Straight Wire.

WOMEN AND FIREARMS
Ihe condition of terror to which the sight of a
mouse, a coc' roach, or a pistol reduces a woman
is phenomenal; yet the same frail creature can
stand the ordeal of a marriage ceremony or a
chri.-teniug without the slightest embarrassment,
while the natural protector of ,l tlie sex,” who
under ordinary circumstances looks big and talks
bigger, cannot face cither of the two last mentioned
trials without feeling fifty ways at once, and
expiriencing a sensation that causes him to
devoutly wish that a good healthy earthquake
would come along and swallow up ail that is left
of his diminished former self. But show the
average woman a gun or a pistol, and she will
shrink away and cry “Oh, dear,” and “Oh,
don’t,’’and “ Oh, for mercy sake,”and will tremble
like a leaf even though the weapon be without
lock, stock, or barrel. With a man however—
how different! He will take up the shooting-iron
with as much nonchalance as he would sample a
first-class brand of whiskey, cock it, peer into the
muzzle with a critical expression in his eye, try
the spring of the trigger, and—finish a good pay
ing job for the doctor or the undertaker.
There can be no doubt about it; man in number-
less ways shows his superiority over woman.
Seriously speaking, though, the opportunity of
women, and with it her bravery and fortitude,
comes in the hour when trouble or sickness affects
those who are dear to her. Then it is that she
becomes a ministering angel, in whose keeping the
well-being and safety of a household depends, and
no prudent housewife ever neglects to keep in
stock a bottle or two of St. Jacobs Oil ready for
use in the event of accident or disease overtaking
either herself, the breadwinner, or one of the olive
branches of the family. A great trait in woman’s
character is that she is ever ready to praise the
bridge that carries her over—hence the following
unsolicited testimonial from Mrs. Calvert, of
Darlington: “I have suffered a great deal from
rheumatics in my hands and shoulders. I have
tried a great many things for it, but have found
nothing but St. Jacobs Oil to do me any good.”
This renowned medicine may be had of any
druggist or storekeeper.

FACTS THAT ASTONISH!
People can’t understand how it is done, yet the
patent fact remains that there is nothing im-
possible about it. This reflection is the outcome
of an announcement by the famed Waterbury
Watch Company that to their former triumphs in
the manufacture of thoroughly reliable time-
keepers they have added still another. This time
they lead the trade in producing two entirely
new series of watches, in nickel, sterling silver,
and rolled gold, in the following styles: Series
“K,” gentlemen’s size, short-wind, stem-set, six
jewels, compensation balance, enamelled dial
(Roman or Arabic), and all the latest improve-
ments; series “N,” ladies’ size, a beautiful highly-
finished watch, with either full or Chatelaine dial.
The astonishing thing is that these watches, at
once elegant both in design and finish, and
unequalled for accuracy, are now selling in Aus-
tralia at the following astonishingly low prices:
Series “K,” nickel case 275. (id., sterling silver
455., rolled gold (13 years’ guarantee) G3s.; series
“N,” nickel 255., sterling silver 405., rolled gold
555. No one should think of purchasing a watch
without seeing these new wonders. There is
positively nothing in their line that can approach
them.

Uousscau : “ How is it that women enjoy
so much kissing in presence of men?”
According to Coghlan, N.S. Wales has
13,289 full-blood Chinese, and 807 Mongolian half-
castes. Of these, 001 are females, who number
.150 full-blooded and 415 half castes. The great
majority of male Chinese arc between 21 and 55
years ; most of the females are below 13 years,
though there are a good many between 21 and 35
years. Thus the Chinese number nearly 1.20 per
cent, of the N.S.W. population, leaving out the
aborigin s. Long live the leprosy!
Berry, the English hangman, has resigned
owing to the interference of some doctors who
made him hang a long murderer at the end of a
long rope, whereby the defendant's head was
nearly torn off. The doctors made the rope long,
and the man was long naturally, and legal etiquette
forbade Bprry to seize upon a short man who
might have fitted the situation and hang him
instead, and the execution was a misfit all round.
A cheerful cablegram from Hanover (Germany)
tells of another execution which went wrong
there, but nothing is said of that executioner
resigning—possibly he is a coarse, thick-skinned
person. The knife of the local guillotine somehow
failed to remove the head in this instance, sj the
gentleman in charge seized the partially-severed
criminal and dragged him apart amid much bad
language from the crowd. The crowd has very
liitle sympathy for an embarrassed hangman.
Three young men have developed in Bris’
bane, Q.. a new method of cashing forged cheques.
Having obtained a blank cheque, the name of a
“good mark” would be.forged by No. 1. Next,
they would select some hotel, near which there
was a shop doing a daily cash trade-say, a
butcher's. The forged cheque would be carefully
crossed and marked “Bank.” Next, No. 2would
endorse the cheque, forging the name of the
butcher for that purp. se ; he would then scribble
a note somewhat to this effect : “ Dear Bung,—
Am short of change; kindly cash accompanying
cheque which I have endorsed. —Yours, Bonks.
Then No. 3, in his shirt-sleeves, with a shop-apron
on, and a stub of a pencil over one ear, would
hurry over to Boniface, who would fall into the
trap every time. The trio of swindlers have since
fallen into the “ traps ” themselves.
At Launceston, Tas., a man was summoned
for having used abusive language.. The plaintiff
and a string of witnesses swore to having heard
him use certain very queer expressions. His
lawyer did not put him in the box, but stood him
up on end in Court, nice and clean and angelically
smiling, and, pointing at him with the right
index-finger, said—“ Your worship", I ask you,
as men of the world, as experts in physiognomical
science, which, from the nature of your daily
duties, you, being men of understanding, must be
—I ask you, your worships, if you think it likely,
nay, even if you think it possible, that such a
nice-looking old gentleman, whose features beam
with benevolence and human sympathy, whose
least expression is a benediction and a sermon —if
such a gentleman could use the language attri-
buted to him?” Discharged, without a stain upon
his character.

A member of the Free Kirk in a Scotch
town recently presented his particular kirk with a
stained-glass window, depicting the four Evan-
gelists on the outer margin and leaving a blank
space for future consideration. An elder, asked
what they were likely to put there* replied,
“ Weel, it’sjist a toss-up atween Dawvitt Simp-
son, oor auld me.nister, an’ oor Saviour.”
A Melbourne suburban Mayor called a
benefit meeting for the unemployed and headed
the list with .£5 ss. Two evenings afterwards, in
the tame hall, he gave a public ball which cost
him <£3oo.
Of the Indian Zenana Mission to which
Australia contributes money and lady evangelists,
an authority says:—“A Hindoo convert loses
caste, and an Indian woman who has lost her
caste in nine cases out of ten becomes a prostitute.”
“ When the bullock has fallen, how many
butchers there are ! ”
Dear Bulletin, —Do you know that up
in tropical Queensland, where the sugar-cane
flourisheth and the mosquito peacefully roosts on
the alligator’s tongue, the niggers who form the
night-shift on the sugar-mills always bathe in
molasses preparatory to commencing duties, in
order to keep the dragon-souled pest from biting
their naked bodies ?—-The Real Mackav.

Head. —‘ A Golden
Slianty/
"Tambaroora Jim."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He never drew a sword to fight a dozen foes alone,
iNoi gave a life to save a life no better than In's
own.
Ht) lived because be had been born—the hero of
uiy song—
And fought the battle witii his fist whene’er he
fought a wrong.
Yet there are many men who would do anything
for him—
.A simple chap as went by name of “ Tarnbaroora
Jim.”
He used to keep a shanty in the “ Come-and-find-
it scrub,”
And there were few but knew the name of Tam-
baroora's pub.
He wasn t great in lambing-down, as many land-
-1 irds are,
And never was a man loss fit to stand behind a
bar—
Off-hand, as most bush natives are, and freckled,
tall, and slim,
A careless native of the land was “Tarnbaroora
J mi.”
hen people said that loafers took the profit from
his pub.,
Hed ask them how they thought a chap could do
( without his grub ?
Hed say, “ I’ve gone for days myself without a
bite or sup-
Oh ! I’ve been thro’ the mill and know what ’tis
to be hard-up.”
He might have made his fortune, but he wasn’t in
the swim,
For no one had a softer heart than “ Tarnbaroora
Jim.”
One dismal day I tramp’d across the Come-and-
find-it Flats
A\ith “Ballarat Adolphus,” and a mate of
; Ballarat’s;”
Twas nearly night and raining fast, and all our
things were damp,
We’d no tobacco, and our legs were aching with
the tramp;
We couldn't raise a cent., and so our lamp of hope
was dim ;
And thus we struck the shanty kept by “ Tam-
baroora J im,”
We dropped our swags beneath a tree and squatted
in despair,
But Jim came out to watch the rain, and saw us
sitting there ;
He came and mutter’d, “I suppose you haven’t
half-a-crown,
But come and get some tucker, and a drink to
wash it down.”
And so we took our blueys up and went along
with him,
And then we knew why bushmen swore by “ Tam-
baroora Jim.”
We sat beside his kitchen fire and nursed our tired
knees,
And blessed him when we heal'd the rain go
rushing thro’ the trees.
He made us stay, altho’ he knew we couldn’t
raise a bob,
And tuckered us until we rose some money on a
job.
And many times since then we’ve filled our glasses
to the brim,
And drunk, in many pubs., the health of “ Tam-
baroora Jim.”
A man need never want a meal while Jim had
“ junk ” to carve,
For “ Tarnbaroora” always said a fellow couldn’t
starve.
And this went on until he got a bailiff in his pub.,
Thro’ helping chaps as couldn’t raise the money
for their grub.
And so, one rainy evening, as the distant range
grew dim,
He humped his bluey from the Flats- did “Tam-
baroora Jim.”
I miss the fun in Jim’s old bar—the laughter and
the noise,
The jolly hours I used to spend on pay-nights
witii the boys.
But that’s all past, and “ vain regrets” are useless,
I’ll allow;
They say the Come-and-.find-it Flats are all de-
serted now.
Poor “ Tarnbaroora’s ” dead, perhaps, but that’s
all right with him,-
Saint Peter cottons on to chaps like “Tarnbaroora
Jim.”
I trust that lie and I may meet where starry fields
are grand,
And “liquor up” together in the pubs, in spirit-
land.
But if you chance to drop on Jim while in the
West, my lad,
You won’t forget to tell him that I want to see
,him bad.
I want to shake his hand again—l want to shout
for him—
I want to have a glass or two with “ Tarnbaroora
Jim.”
HENRY LAWSON.
Sydney, March, 1892.

DECLARATION
THOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 515 Gcorge-st.,
Sydney, in the colony of New South Wales, do hereby
solemnly and sincerely declare as follows ;
Whereas, I suffered very severely from a very
troublesome affection of the Nei*V6S, which affected me so
much that I was quite unable to attend to my business.
I tried several Doctors and many Remedies, but all of
no avail. Whereas, I, as a last hope obtained some of
Herr Rassmussen’s PURELY HERBAL
“ DENMARK UNIVERSAL PILLS,’' which
I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COMPLETELY
CURED ME within a few weeks, and although this is
over five years ago, I have remained well ever since.
The DENMARK UNIVERSAL PILLS also cured
my wife, and I make this solemn declaration, conscien-
tiously believing the same to be true.
THOS. MUDIMAN.
Subscribed and declared at Sydney, this 23rd day of
November, 1891, before me,
S. T. Whiddok,

Extract from a Salvation preacher’s speech
at Hamilton (M.L.) ; -
Friends, yer pays fourpuns a pound for yer mutton,
sixpuns a pound for yer beef, and here we are offerin’ yer
the lamb of Oord for nothin’. And may tho Lord bless
the reading of His word.
The most productive farm in Australasia
must surely be that of John Grigg, Canterbury
(M.L.). Its area is 10,000 acres. The average
wheat crop is 230,000 bushels, and the average
stock run comprise. 12,000 sheep, 700 cattle, 500
horses, and 1000 pigs. Besides that he fattens
15,000 bought sheep annually, and during the last
two years he has sent 10,000 frozen lambs to Lon-
don. He employs 150 men who bank £l5OOO of
their savings every year. During the last three
years he has paid an annual average of JEISCO in
railway-freight. This wonderful farm is known
hroughout Canterbury as the Land of Goshen.

Apropos of the conviction of Smith, alias
Adams, for distributing swindling sweep-circulars bear-
ing no imprint, G. H. Reid, for defendant, asked in court
why the police didn’t also prosecute the genuine
Adams whose circulars likewise bore no imprint?
The fact is the Law tacitly recognises the Australian
sweep-maker.

% # *
Embezzlements, filicides, and other “ police
items” traceable to gambling have very much decreased
in Maoriland since the legalisation of the totaljaator,
which, by the way, is now taxed there, and the pioceeds
devoted to charitable purposes. Sir Julius Vogel made
his proposal to derive a revenue from the “ tote,” on the
suggestion of the present writer. And apropos of this,
Tub Bulletin cannot understand why the proprietary
race-clubs do not in their own interest agitate for the
introduction of the belting-machine in N.S.W. At an
ordinary Hosehill, Mooreficld, or Warwick Farm meeting
from £15,000 to £20,000 is betted. The 10 per cent,
deduction made by tire clubs—as in M.L.—would give
from £l5OO to £2OOO eacli meeting, which would enable
these clubs to quadruple their stakes and contribute
besides about £2OO a meeting to charities, without hurt-
ing themselves lit the slightest. Big stakes mean
“trying” horses and the extinction of the domination
of “Johnny Armstrong ” and of the mushroom “book.”
Then again, as the “tote” may only he used on the
course, persons wishing to have their “ bit on ” must see
the races—so the attendance is increased. It is possible
to raise thousands a year for charity in any Australian
province by legalising the “ tote.”

The luck of the S.A. Advertiser (Ade-
laide) dates from the wreck of the Gothenburg on
the Barrier Reef, nearly a score of years ago.
B. de Lissa, an ex-’TISER reporter (now a Borneo
planter) was at the W'reck and telegraphed full
particulars to his old office, which thus got clean
ahead of its rival, and achieved in one act a
reputation for enterprise. The lowering of the
’Tiskii’s price to the popular penny, as against
the Register’s “tuppence,” clinched its good
fortune.
Montreal (Canada) paragraph :
An inspector said that, after long vainly trying to find
out how liquor was sold on Sundays in the French
quarters of the city, one of his men.‘whilst in a candy
store, saw a customer pass the landlord some money.
The proprietor then produced a small rubber tube from
under his vest, one end of which the man put in his
mouth, and sucked. The officer pounced oh the pro-
prietor, and a search revealed that he wore a pair of tin
corsets, with a space between the inner and outer
partitions, capable of holding over a gallon of liquor. To
this the tube was attached ; the customers, leaning over
the counter took it in their mouths, and sucked until the
proprietor thought tlifrv had had their money’s worth,
when I lie supply was turned off, the tube being put back
underneath the vest.
