Somebody up North sends us again the
old, old story about the new churn who put up at
a country pub, and wore a lot of glittering gems
cn his fore leg, and got his crested correspondence
presented to him on a tray by the rouseabout, and
was generally understood to be something incog.
Then he decided, as usual, to buy a station, and
the proprietor thereof drove him round in a
buggy, and liquored him, and introduced him to
his daughters, and lent him .£5 till he could
“wire to his bankers,’’and next morning his room
was empty and the publican was raving about an
unpaid bill and a garish portmanteau with a
boulder in it. Same old story all through.
A new race of troglodytes is growing up
along the Darling, (N.S.W.) —a race that camps
in holes or underneath trees, and lives miserably
on fish and rabbits and anything that happens to
drift down the river. These are in part the
unemployed of Sydney— the men who were sent
out by the Labour Bureau on free passes, and who,
being unable to walk back, vs ill probably camp
wretchedly by the Darling until they relapse into
barbarism and begin to go naked and to run after
’possums: the rest of them are mostly men who
were sacked to make room for the cheap Labour
Bureau crowd from the metropolis. Still the
unemployed keep decreasing in Sydney, and that
is probably all that the Bureau was created for.
Dear Bulletin, —Are you fully aware of
the awful truth! n your observation in last week’s
leader that “the rich (“at Home”) were able to
lend out at interest the money which should have
bought tables and chairs for the poor,” or that
many of the poor—in Ireland, at least—are without
tables and chairs? 1 remember how, in my young
school-days, when enjoying the hospitality of a
youth named Cody, we used to squat on the floor
and eat potatoes piled upon an old door which
was unhinged and laid low for the occasion under
the roof of a mud cabin in the shade of one of
the wild hills of Tipperary. Cody was sent to
America; I to a priestly college—afterwards to
drift to Australia. Macliae's etching in “Brunt's
Rdiques ” representing the “ boys ” playing cauls
on a coffin “ the night before Lany was.stretched ”
might have been sketched from life. The coffin
comes in very handily on cold nights when it
would bo uncomfortable to take down the door,
as many can tell you who, like myself, have come
from poor, poverty-stricken Ireland.—Yours, B.
Old Ireland’s wherever
Her sons may endeavour,
No matter the land we were born in.
So, strike up, my jew'l, and
Hurroo for Home Rule and
St. Patrick’s Day in the morning.
RORY O'BATHERSHIN.

Australia’s Chinese
Legacy.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Leprosy entailed a hideous excommunica-
tion in the ancient times of the HebreWs, a social
ostracism that made its victim an unconditionally
prohibited pariah, on whom had fallen the curse
of heaven and the still more cruel ban of man.
Now-a-days, with the prevalence of juster and
more humane ideas, leprosy, in Australian com-
munities at all events, is tenderly dealt with, and
means, at its worst, a comparatively luxurious
quarantine tempered with the indulgences that
pity prescribes to each death-doomed life-recluse.
Beyond having proved the bacillary nature of
leprosy, physicians know absolutely nothing about
it except that the condition of a leper may be
palliated. Inthepresentstateof medicalknowledge,
leprosy is incurable. Members of the facultydispute
even as to whether it is contagious. The microbe
has been isolated, but all efforts to “cultivate”
it, or keep it alive for any decent period of time,
nave failed. According to the late Morell
Mackenzie, “ the number of lepers now dying by
mches throughout the world must be reckoned by
millions.” Leprosy is a strange, old, fossil disease
uterly unlike any other known to science—a
prehistoric complaint that has remained over
rom antediluvian days, like one stray mammoth
ett to wander the earth alone when all the rest
are dead.
Hitherto, leprosy has been classed as a disease
most exclusively confined to Chinamen and
man coolies. It is high time to spring a fresh
arm when it is discovered that Australian
«ati Ve whites—natives, too, who have never left
amongst our lepers.
eirrJi A zare^. at kittle Bay, Sydney, contained
mgtit Australians when I visited it, the other day,
a 21 lepers, all told. Two of these
w ght were females.

villacr,. ■ i K i ( -nclosure, there is a miniature

Arts ” °T S °’ facetiously styled the “ School of
inside the lep^r outages stand just
the f act . P lO !’ th( ' r . s,x are ranged along
a slight elevation ODDOsite. In tho
hollow between is seen a luxuriant melon-patch,
while away to the left is a vegetable garden.
Two lepers are gardening in the distance,
and by no means doing a lazy stroke. Flowers
bloom gaily in the little patches of flower-garden
in front of each cottage across the hollow. On
the place itself is impressed no Mosaic sign what-
ever of a “fretting leprosy.”
* * #
Entering the cottage near the gate, and
to the left, it is found to accommodate three
lepers, It is halved off, and the plan consists (in
each half) of a front sitting-room and a back bed-
room—one of the latter containing two beds.
Everything is scrupulously, even painfully, clean.
A big-nosed, swollen-lipped, face-punctured
man is in the first sitting-room, doing some fancy-
work of ornamentation to the window. He just
turns his head and nods, and then proceeds again
lethargically with his task. I have seen him
before—in a pantomime. I feel sure the super’s
mask-makers get their models from lazarets. I
thought this poor, handsomely-hideous patient
was a Chinee, so distorted were his features. I
was wrong. He is a N.S.W. native, and has
never been out of the province. He has been a
carrier, a shearer, a fencer, a mail-coach driver, a
bush-carpenter—in fact, anything and everything.
He has been married, and his wife bore him one*
child, who lived for a few hours. He had his
headquarters at Walgett for nine years, and at
Moree for seven years. At a shearing time ho
stabbed the base of the left thumb with the
shears, and three weeks afterwards noticed that
the whole of his left-hand was numb. Later, he
found himself liable to injure that hand without
feeling any pain ; in this manner, he burnt the
thumb deeply with a soldering-iron, and the little
finger with a lighted cigar, discovering these facts
otherwise than by a sensation of pain. About
five years later, he ran a nail into the sole of the
left foot, and the wound ulcerated, being scarcely
yet (nearly three years later) healed. The foot
swelled and remained swollen. His left hand and
forearm lost sensation, until one night, when a
burning log rolled out of his camp-fire against
that arm, he knew nothing of it until the
upper arm began to burn. Two years aeo,
he was much exposed to floods and wet; he caught
cold and has been hoarse ever since. The right
foot next began swelling, and later the hands.
About eleven months ago his face began to distort
itself, until now his nose is so swollen as to
entirely hide its oriffyial shape ; both cheeks are
thickened, one more than the other, giving the
mask a lopsided cant; the whole being a curious
colour-blend of brown, red, and blue. His is
tubercular leprosy.
Such is the first acknowledged leper I ever
gazed upon. There is nothing in Ids known his-
tory to indicate that he was more intimate with
Chinese than the general run of bushmen. For
eight months of last year he earned his living by
catching fish—leprosy is believed by some doctors
to be connected with, or to be at leaQt sympathetic
with, a fish diet—but that was patently long after
the disease was well established in him. He was
admitted to the Leper Hospital in December last.
On the table of the sitting-room which he ap-
parently shares with another, but invisible,
patient—most of the lepers vanish mysteriously
into recesses on the intrusion of an unknown
visitor—are some books. The title of the top one
stares at me boldly ; it is—“ The Ghouls of Gal-
veston : or, Deadwood Dick, junr., in Texas.”
Even a leper can, in imagination, perform deeds
nf fire blood, heroism —and Love !
An easy-chair is disposed on the verandah, and
the whole cottage has an air of comfort about it
that constantly lulls one into a careless frame of
mind, but this must be guarded against, lest one
absent-mindedly take up a book, touch a bed
stead, or wall, or anything consecrated to leprosy'
* » #
Crossing the hollow, cucumbers galore are
seen to be rotting in the weather. Their crop has
been too prolific this year. Huge watermelons
sprawl around every where, giving no sign of their
leprous environment.
A Chinese leper suffering from the mutilating
form of the disease consents to show me his hands,
the finger-joints of which are slowly disappearing,
while the hands themselves are paralysed and
permanently flexed, the patient being unable to
straighten out his fingers. He says nothing in
answer to questions, but stands dumbly exhibiting
his paws, and trots away out of sight so soon as he
gets a chance to do so.
* * #
Here is a boy leper, a native of Balmain,
Sydney, not yet 1G years of age. He, too, is
suffering from the mutilating form of leprosy. His
fingers are hopelessly flexed on to the palm,
though, with assistance, the first two fingers on
each hand can be extended out at right-angles
with the palms, the other fingers remaining
paralysed. Plis face is slightly swollen and of a
reddish brown colour, witli a weak redness around
the eyes and an apparent absence of eyelashes.
His father and mother were both Australian
natives; his grandmother on the father’s side was
also a native, her husband having been English.
The grandparents on the mother’s side were Irish.
The patient was one of nine children. Phthisis
and bronchitis seem to affect most of the family
and one is wasting away. This one always lived
in some Sydney suburb, with the exception of a
short period during which he visited Melbourne.
His father owned a little property, and drank
himself into a lunatic asylum, where he is still
confined. His mother, now aged over 50 years,
enjoys good health. The family habitually bought
vegetables from Chinese, but had no other special
communications with the pigtail-wearers. The
patient attended a State school from the usual
age. He slept with his mother, because he was
afraid o f the dark. He was fond of fishing and of
fish. His mother, still robust and healthy, for a
long time habitually dressed his sores.
When this youth was 12 years old, he acquired
some water-blisters on his left hand ; these broke
and left small ulcers, by which he lost two finger-
nails ; the ulcers healed, but contraction of the
fore and middle fingers followed, after which there
set in a wasting of the forearm, and his fingers
cracked. The following year his left heel
hardened and secreted offensive matter, and body
symptoms followed each other with great rapidity.
He has grown three or four inches since he has
been in the lazaret. He declares that ho hasn’t
the slightest idea as to how he first became
diseased.
# • #
Turning into “The School of Arts,” it is
found to have been hastily deserted. Even the
attractions of a half-played game of dominoes
were not sufficient to counteract the scare of an
unknown visitor. The “ School of Arts ”is a big
apartment, furnished with two long tables, a
proscenium curtain for private theatricals, and
cheerful pictures on the walls. At the back are
three big baths, with hot and cold water connec-
tions : six lavatory basins, &c.; large common
wash-house possessing a monster washing machine,
&0., &c. The ‘ School of Arts ” is the recreation
room of the lepers. Half-a-dozen of them were
here but a moment ago, but they vanished like
spooks out at the back as we came in at the front.
# # #
Another mutilated Chinaman is prevailed
upon to display his hideous hands. The fingers on
both are missing—they have rotted off mysteriously
right to the stumps, only about half-an-inch of
each stump being left on the swollen, soft, dark-
brown, puffy hands. A curious fact is that,
though the flesh and bone and all of the fingers
have dropped off, the half-inch of each stump still
bears fragments of bastard finger-nail, as if the
disease, while eating up the fingers, had drawn
the nails right up to the knuckles.
Another Chinaman exhibits a tubercular con-
dition of the face, which, all puffed and blotched
as it is, with thickened and broadened nose and
swollen eyes, and distorted neck, gives him that
kind of leonine appearance which satirical carica-
turists attribute to John Bull. In addition to
this general aspect, he has one eye almost closed,
blubber lips, and thick lumps under the ears,
while a decorative plaster of scabs shields his
spread-out nose.
A good-looking, black-haired, smart young
Chinaman reveals to a non-professional observer
little or nothing of his leprotic condition ; but his
hands are going and he is seen to be stiff and
cramped below the knees.
Now comes a Mongolian horror of the knobby
variety. His face could give a twisted apple-tree-
bough points for knobbiness. The rich brown
skin stands out in hard clusters like groups of
walnuts ; eye-surroundings puffed, with bloodshot
balls; lips thickened and swollen like the fanciful
style of a burnt-cork minstrel; not a flat, smooth
spot on the whole of the countenance; nose
broadened and twisted with horribly gaping
nostrils —and, yet, an undefinable glint of merri-
ness undercurrent of all! Yes, he was all right, he
had no pain, he was never so comfortably off in his
life, he might as well die by leprosy’s slow stages
as of any swifter complaint.
“ How long you been sick ?”
“Befo’”—slowly counting off on his dropsical
fingers—“befo’ one, two, tlee —’um—befo’ ten
moon.”
“ He means that he has been 10 months here,”
explained the medical superintendent. “He
must have been leprotic about a couple of years.”
“ You like go China again ?”
“Yes; go China by-’n-bye.”
“You go fishing?”
“Yes, plenty catchy fish sometimes.”
The Government supply these poor wretches
with hooks and lines and they are allowed to fish
along a certain distance of the shore—rocks chiefly
• —the boundary-lines being marked by rigged-up
ropes.
“ Plenty good flower gardens?”
“Yes, plentee cabbagee, too muchee young
fellah; too muchee coocumbee, altogethee go
bad.”
“ You like this place ? ’
“ Oli, nicee place.”
And the poor knobby-faced scarecrow strode off
to his private lair.
“ Charley ” is a swell kanaka, a Tanna Islander.
His little cottage is full of nick-nacks, and owns
wall-pictures, handsomely framed, while the floor
is covered with a gaudy oil-cloth. There is a
chest of drawers, with a grand looking-glass, and
Charlie is seated on the bed in a startlingly white
shirt, stiff with starch, a masher collar, and a

any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-

Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the

gels the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by Tim Bulletin ; I860 words go to,a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don't write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. 11 Boil it down."

gay-coloured necktie, black cloth waistcoat and
pants. He has doffed his coat on account of the
heat. Charlie is vain of his room, and if you
compliment him upon its appearance, he will love
you—for the time being. As he sits with folded
arms, one can see nothing much the matter with
him, but when he exhibits his hands, the work of
the terrible plague is visible. Again, the fingers
self-corroded down to stumps ; and again the nails
growing only an inch from the main knuckles of
the hand. Doctors don’t attempt to explain this
phenomenon.
Charlie has a green-painted stand for flowerpots
out on his verandah, which is reached by a flight
of steps ; also a model —three feet or so—of a line-
of-battle ship ; and is, in Lis way, as happy as a
king. His present great grief is—“ Too much
sea-breeze longa flowers there; die, all die, longa
wind.”
There are other male lepers, whites and Chinese,
but they have retreated to their private apart-
ments, or are concealed around the grounds.
* # #
All of the white lepers are believed to be
natives of N.S.W. One man, who belonged to a
family of seven, is now about 31 years of age.
After his school-years, lie was one year with a
cordial-maker, after which he helped his mother,
who kept a restaurant, his especial duty being to
buy vegetables in tbc markets —frequently from
Chinese.
The exact period when he absorbed the leprotic
germs is untraceable. At 17 he became a carter,
and shortly afterwards began to learn plastering,
his first work being to beat up bullock’s-hair and
mortar with a trowel. When getting on for 20,
the trowel broke the skin over the outer side of
the right fore-finger, which swelled and has never
since recovered its natural size or appearance.
Anaesthesia of hands and feet set in, and he would
frequently burn or injure these limbs without
feeling it. When about 24, he had an attack des-
cribed as erysipelas and rheumatic fever, from
which followed general swelling and distortion of
his hands and feet. Two years later his eyes
became diseased and he almost lost his sight.
In ’BB, he tried to cut his throat. In August of
that year he was admitted- to the lazaret. His
father died insane in Gladesville Asylum,
* # #
A youth of 19 years is a native of the
Mudgee district, N.S.W. His father was also
native-born, of Irish descent ; his mother was
born at sea, of English parents. Both are still
living and in good health, and to them were born
nine other children, all of whom are in the best of
condition. The family has always lived on the
Meroo—an old goldfield continuously haunted by
Chinese, but the intercourse between the family
and the Chinkies was restricted to purchases of
tea and sugar, and sometimes of boots from a
Chinese storekeeper. Fish was a rare article of
diet with this patient, who, until 13 years old,
was well, strong, intelligent and active. Then,
the fingers of the left hand began to contract, and
the muscles of the forearm to waste; blebs appeared
on the fingers, and loss of sensation followed. At
15, reddish-brown and pure brown spots appeared
on the trunk ; the right hand and right foot con-
tracted and swelled; and at 16, the face gob
discoloured and began to swell and become dis-
figured. There is no clue to the date and manner
of his first absorption of the leprotic virus (if there
be such a virus).
.# . * #
A 30-year-old timber-getter, from the
.Richmond River district, with tuberculous face,
ulcerated feet, swollen hands, and other symp-
toms, dates his knowledge that he was suffering
from a progressive disease from a time, "about 3|
years ago, when a peg in the sole of a new boot
injured the surface about the root of the great toe;
an ulcer followed, which continually broke out
again, and heralded the other symptoms. In ’B7,
he helped to place a dead leper in a coffin. It was
in ’BB that he injured his toe. He lias been
married nearly ten years, and his wife has borne
him two children. His father was an Alsatian,
and his mother English. There are four others
in the family ; all healthy.
# # #
A native of Gunnedah, N.S.W., now 24
years old--whose chief occupations were fencing,
shearing, and droving, who never had special
communication with Chinese or other Asiatics,
who is not fond of fish and has had only rare
opportunities of eating it fresh, while he has
seldom used the canned or salted article —has his
face and ears distorted shockingly with tubercular
leprosy, while his body is brown-spotted, bis
hands and feet swollen and discoloured. He dates
his symptoms from ’B4, when he had a whitlow,
and lost a joint of the right index-finger.
# * *
Outside the enclosure, away up on the
hill, in a snug little isolated cottage, reside the
two female lepers. Each has a cosy sitting-room
and a bedroom, and there is an easy lounge and
an easy chair on the verandah, where we find
both patients resting themselves. One lies on the
lounge, with her once evidently pleasant face
swollen and discoloured into a strong contrast with
her iron-grey hair; her hands and feet, too, are
badly swollen. She is a widow, 53 years of age,
and has borne six children. Her husband was
accidentally killed in ’7O. To support her children,
she traded in haberdashery in a small way. She
habitually dealt with Chinese for vegetables, but
never had closer communication with them or
with other Asiatics. She does not remember ever
having seen another leper previous to her entry
into the lazaret. She was never fond of fish. She
-has only been a few weeks at Little Bay.
The veiled lady in the easy chair is unmarried,
and aged 34 years. Her case is heroic. She
nursed a younger brother through years of leprosy
and saw him deposited in the grave in ’B9. About
’B3, this lady had a sore heel, attributed to the
pressure of a tight boot, but after three weeks the
place healed perfectly. Next year, discoloured
spots were seen on the legs, and red spots on the
face; later, brown, or reddish-brown spots and
patches on the body ; in ’B7 she lost her eyebrows;
in ’B9, her voice became affected, and it is now
confined to a whisper. Her state is tuberculous
and anaesthetic. She has been at Little Bay about
a year, now.
The origin of her brother’s disease is not known.
He was a manly fellow, sft. llin. in height, but
of a weakly constitution, and seems to have
suffered ill-health from boyhood upwards. Their
father and mother were both natives of Ireland ;
Srand parents —Scotch on the father’s side and
Inglish on the mother’s. The leper was the
youngest son of seven living. He was born
in ’6l at Campbelltown (N.S.W.), where he
lived till ’6B; he then resided in Sydney till ’7O;
in Adelaide (S.A.) till ’BO, when he returned to
Sydney, where he died in ’B9.
* * *
Here, then, in an enclosed and cultivated
area embracing a little over two and a quarter
acres, the afflicted lepers known to the N.S.W*
Health authorities doze out their monotonous
existence, softened and cheered by the modern
conveniences necessary to comfort and cleanliness,
and treated, after a fashion, as pets. In addition
to many papers and periodicals provided by
Government, the lazaret receives frequent contri-
butions of reading matter. The Chinese lepers, of
course, must content themselves with looking at
the pictures.
There are 21 lepers at present in the lazaret—l9
men and two women. The nationalities arc as
follows Chinese, 11 ; N.S.W. natives, 8 ;
Javanee, 1 ; South Sea Islander, 1. On Ist
January, ’9l, the tally was 13 8 Chinese, 4
N.S.W., 1 Javanee, One Chinaman and one
N.S. Welshman died during the year. Live
N.S.W. natives, four Chinese, and one South Sea
Islander were admitted during the year —a total
of 10 out of 17 eases suspected of leprosy.
Since the establishment of tin l lazaret in ’B3,
there have been 31 patients admitted Chinese,
19 (8 died) ; natives of N.S.W., 9 (1 died) ; and 1
half-caste West Indian (discharged with healed
sores in ’BS, there being then no law in existence
to warrant his detention); one Javanee, and one
Kanaka.
After much trouble and many representations to
the late Farkes Government, the latter was induced
to pass a Leprosy Act in November, ’9O, which gave
the sanitary authorities full powers to deal with
lepers and suspected lepers, and made it incum-
bent on all medical men, under a penalty of “ not
less than .£lO nor more than to report sus-
pected cases to the authorities without delay. It
is to the operation of this Act that the compara-
tively large number of suspects examined last year
is due.
Two wardsmen look after the lepers. The head
wardsman, who has been a soldier, though still
young and active man, has had considerable ex-
perience in field hospitals and dispensaries, and
handles the lepers fearlessly, seeming to be quite
at home in his repulsive tasks. The best dis-
infectant against leprosy is held to be corrosive
sublimate ; carbolic acid and other approved dis-
infectants are inert, unless they are made of such
concentrated strength that, they could not be
personally handled. The corrosive sublimate is
found to be effective in the proportion of 1 part
in 1000,
# * *
Of the South Coast Hospital itself, I do
not intend to say much. It is the great sewer of
the infectious diseases of Sydney. All cases of
erysipelas, measles, scarlet fever, diphtheria,
chicken-pox, whooping-cough, typhoid fever, &c.,
have to be reported to the authorities; the
Medical Board of Health telephones at once to
Little Bay, and off starts an ambulance to the
locality named. There are four ambulances, but
not one specially reserved for lepers ! When an
ambulance is used for the conveyance of any
leper, it is carefully disinfected with sulphur and
corrosive sublimate.
The medical superintendent, to whom thanks is
due for courtesies tendered, is Dr. W. Peirce, a
reserved, hermit-like sort of man, wrapped up in
scientific breedings, and whose special forte is
said to be the treatment of typhoid fever. I
gathered by hint rather than by avowal that Dr,
I’oirce does not rely much on the hydropathic, or
“ wet-pack ” system of cure. He has super-
intended Little Bay for six years.
* * *
The moral of a visit is that, though the
immediate leprotic inoculation of whites is almost
invariably obscure, seeing that the insidious
disease often takes years to incubate—lies hidden
until a scratch or a bruise calls forth its venomous
powers—leprosy is a curse that entered Australia
with the Chinaman. Its discovery upon white's
should furnish another reason why the yellow
man should bo banished to Lis own leprous laud
Heaven knows how many whites now-a-days are
mixing with their Australian fellows freely,
having the seeds of leprosy latent in their blood
It is possible that a man of strong vitality might
bo a medium of transmission to others, and yet
never disclose the disease on himself to himself
This last is my own speculation, unsupported bv
mooted theories, and with it I bad better clm£
the chapter. e

Jim’s Whip.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Yes, there it hangs upon the wall
And never gives a sound,
The hand that trimmed its greenhidc fall
Is hidden underground,
There, in that patch of sallee shade,
Beneath that grassy mound.
I never take it from the wall,
That whip belonged to him.
The man I singled from them all,
He was my husband, Jim ;
I see him now, so straight and tall,
>So long and lithe of limb.
That whip was with him night and day
When he was on the track ;
I’ve often heard him laugh, and say
That when they heard its crack,
After the breaking of the drought,
1 he cattle all came back.*
And all the time that Jim was here,
A-working on the run,
I’d hear that whip ring sharp and clear
Just about set of sun,
To let me know that he was near
And that his work was done.
I was away that afternoon,
Penning the calves, when, bang !
I heard his whip, ’twas rather soon—
A thousand echoes rang
And died away among the hills,
As toward the hut I sprang.
I made the tea and waited, but,
Seized by a sudden whim,
I went and sat outside the hut
Watching the light grow dim—
I waited there till after dark,
But not a sign of Jim.
The evening air was damp with dew ;
Just as the clock struck ten
His horse came riderless—l knew
What was the matter then.
M by should the Lord have singled out
My Jim from other men ?
I took the horse and found him, where
He lay beneath the sky,
With blood all clotted on his hair ;
I felt too dazed to cry—-
I held him to me as I prayed
To God that I might die.
But sometimes now I seeni to hear
Just when the air grows chill
A single whip-crack, sharp and clear,-
Re-echo from the hill,
J hat s Jim, to let me know he’s neat
And thinking of me still.
BARCROFT H. BOAKE

the cattle home after the drought."
( Dear Bulletin, — \our correspondent
G.J.S. having' dragged my name into Ids com-
munication on Wetere te Rerenga in this week’s
issue of The Bulletin, permit me to say that
“G.J;S.”Is wrong in most of his facts, that heriefeer
saw or conversed with Te Rerenga, and that he
Was never in the Ngatimaniapoto Country in his
life. Te Rerenga, although a bully, a coward ard
a murderer, was never a cannibal (Kai tangata
Maori), an eye-swallower or a Halt Hau. He was
certainly in anils against us in the old Waitara
campaign, but for years before, and after, he was
a peaceful trader, and commanded Ids own little
schooner, the Parahanini, which ran regularly
between Kawhia and Taranaki with fruit and
potatoes; “ G.J.S.” asserts that Te Rerenga, or
NV etete, as he was known in those days, murdered
my grandfather, the Rev; John Whiteley, and
drank his blood. The first statement I believe
to bo untrue, although as leader of the
tana or war-party lie was morally the
murderer, and as such would have paid the
penalty for his crime on two occasions, but that
he was assisted by the Native Ministers of the
time to escape the vengeance of relatives and the
provisions of the law. The second statement is a
lib 1 know you approve of plain speaking—con-
ceived in the worst possible, taste and published in
the interests of mere newspaper sensationalism.
My grandfather was shot (as I believe, by a
European)! the other victims of the White Cliffs
affair, including a young mother and her little
children, were savagely slain with a tomahawk.
“G.J.S.” exhibits his ignorance of Maoriland
history by stating that in 1884 he met Wetere,
who “had then been under, the ban of outlawry
for 20 years.” Te Wetere was proclaimed an out-
law in 1860 for the White Cliffs affair, was par-
doned by Sir Donald M’Lean in 1875, met Sir
George Grey at Waitara in 1878, and had been a
frequent visitor to Auckland since that date, so
that this imaginary . conversation between
“G.J.S.” and an outlawed murderer in 1884 is
also mere bunkum. . Wetere’s “numerous and
valorous henchmen ” are the mere creatures of
“G.J.S.’s” imagination, and his “inaccessible”
fortress could have been stormed and taken by ten
men of the N.Z.A.C. any day. Ac the time of
the massacre, Colonel (now General Sir George)
Whitmore could have wiped the Ngatimaniapoto
off the .face of the earth if lie had been allowed a
free hand by the Government of the day; but it was
ever the policy of New Zealand Ministers to allow
native murderers to escape. Finally “G.J.S.’s”
tales of “ Wesley, the Snag,” besides being at
variance with historical fact, are palpably the
inventions of a wretched penny-a-liner, who, in
order to trade off his wretched production under
the guise of personal reminiscences, has libelled a

| Dear Bulletin, — Concerning your recent
| paragraph re heights of Kosciusko and adjacent
j peak : having just completed a survey of this part
i of the Snowy Mountain Range, during winch the
position and relative heights of the various peaks
[ were accurately determined, I am in a position to
state as follows : —Mount Kosciusko, a smooth,
grassy, round-topped hill, on which a trig, station
has lately been erected, is the highest. Muellers
Peak, a mound of huge granite rocks, on which ;i
cairn was erected many years ago, is the second in
height. From the inscription, &c., left by
visitors on this cairn, it is evident that the peak
has frequently been mistaken for Kosciusko. It is
licit, lioH'evel’, situated on the instill rahge, tint on a
ppnr running towards the Murray. Moililt Twy-
wiw: aiiother glassy hill, is the third in height.
Iherg is only a difference Of about 40ft. between
the highest and lowest of these three. Mounts
Kosciusko and Tdwnsolid ale identical, the latter
name having been given by Dr. Von Loildenfeld
who, standing oil Mueller s Peak, which he sup-
posed td be Kosciusko, discovered a higher point
and naitled it as before stated—Yours, W. h’
HowaeiJ, Licensed Surveyor, Survey Camp!

Some months ago the case of Mrs. Mary Cudrb,
was first published. The great public accented
the facts, as they had every reason to do. Others
a very few (professional men perhaps) pretended
to doubt, and sent emissaries to inquire bf tho
lady herself whether her allegations had hot been
added to, or altered, for the sake of popular effect
Here is her answer! She repeats what she at
hrst said, and puts a quietus on all who called
her words in question. It will be observed that
her statement is as plain and solemn as language
can make it. 6
[copy.]
■*■’ Mary Cuddy, of 28, Catherine Street, Rich-
mond Road, Leeds, do solemnly and sincerely
declare as follows y
Ever since I was a girl I have suffered from ill
ness. I always had a pain both before and after
eating, and never seemed able to gain and keen
my strength, and felt that something was pullinl
me down. I had a nasty queer feeling in mv
stomach. Sometimes food seemed to ease it and
it other times it made me feel worse, and often I
went without food, for I was afraid to eat. Com
monly when food was placed before me I could not
touch it, and I often fainted at tho very sight of it
After a while I became so weak I could scarcely
stand or walk. I thought it was consumption
coming on by degrees and I took all sorts of
medicines to try and get relief, but it was of no use
and I got tired of taking physic, for I had lost all
taitli in it. My business was so urgent that I was
compelled to be at work, otherwise I would have
laid m bed, so weak had I become. With the
weakness and loss of appetite there were other
feelings and signs that were bad and alarmed me
greatly. Among them were these A yellowish
colour of the skin and eyes, sometimes a cold
clammy perspiration, pains and aches in the sides’
the chest and back, headache, a kind of wind or gas
coming up into my throat and mouth that was so
sour and sicketlihg I could scarcely bear it, Once in
a while I Would have a strange fluttering and pal-
pitation that made hie think my heart must bo
affected; My heart Would thump so that 1 feared
it would jump out of its place, and I have had to
walk about the room for two or three hours at a
time, for I Could not sit or lie; The pain was so
severe that I have asked my hUsbahd if he could
not heal- my heart thumping as 1 walked about.
I always slept badly at night, and frequently had
horrible dreams, and was so melancholy and
depressed in spirits that 1 Wotlld sit down add
cry, for I got no pleasure as time dragged wearily
by - 1 •'ad so little energy or strength that it was
all 1 could do to summon Courage for the labour
upon which the family (at least in part) depended
for support; I am a dressmaker, and it will be
easily understood how hard my life was, for 1
didn t think it would last much longer; Not long
ago (May, 1887) I made up my mind to try a
medicine that is advertised and known all over
the country. I mean Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup. I had no faith in it at first, for how cah
one believe in What one knows nothing about? I
bought and tried Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup
only because of its reputation. How could so
many people, I asked myself, praise a medicine so
much if it had no virtue? 1 can only say that 1
found what they said to be true. After beginning
with the Syrup, relief soon followed; My food
digested better and gave me strength, and by
persevering with it all my pains disappeared; 1
could eat my food with a relish, and everything
agreed with me. Now and again when, through
confinement and hard work, 1 feel a touch of my
old complaint I take a dose or two of Seigel’s
>Syi up and the trouble goes no further. Since the
publication of my testimonial many persons have
called at my house and asked me if all that is pub-
lished about my ease is true, and if the Proprietors
of Seigel’s Syrup had made additions to my state-
ment.
I told them all that every word was true and
nothing had been added by the Proprietors of the
medicine, but I could add a good deal more, for no
words can describe what my sufferings were
during all those long years. I never expected
being well again in this world. Seigel’s Syrup
saved my life and I desire other sufferers to know
of what did so much for me. I will gladly answer
inquiries.
And I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, by
virtue of the provisions of the Statutory Declara-
tion Act (Will. IV., c. 62.)
_ , , , , (Signed) Mary Cuddy.
Declared before me at Leeds, in the '
County of York, by the said Mary
Cuddy, on Monday, the 10th day of
August, 1891.
(Signed) Alb\ Cooke,
Mayor of Leeds. ,
Not a syllable further is needed except to say
that her ailment, indigestion and dyspepsia,
burdens and saddens the lives of many other
women (and men also), who will read with new
hope the outcome of Mrs. Cuddy’s case, and place
a confidence which no cavilling can shake, in the
remedy which restored to her the health and
happiness that Providence designs for us all.
